APPENDIX  D: Summary  of Fluvial Processes  at Intersection  and 
Proximity  Sites
                                                                                                                                   
  
 
Site 
ID Reach Type At‐risk 
Infrastructure  Description Photo
I‐149 1  I  Pipe  Pipe crosses diagonally  beneath 
channel at downstream  limit of 
down valley‐migrating  
meander,  with slumping 
western bank, at upstream ‐
migrating  knickpoint  with scour 
pool eroded into till. 
P‐070 1  P/I  MH/pipe  Slight meander has slowly 
migrated eastward  and down‐
valley over the buried pipe, but 
a now‐fatigued crib wall, 
constructed  along the eastern 
bank to protect a nearby 
manhole,  has inhibited further 
migration.  
P‐516 1  P  MH/pipe  Sharp meander exhibiting  
prominent  down‐valley and 
slight lateral erosion is 
encroaching  on the buried 
sewer and manhole adjacent to 
the channel.  Its rate of 
migration,  however,  has likely 
slowed due to over‐widening 
and an inward thalweg shift at 
the bend.  
I‐150 1  I  Pipe  Pipe crosses diagonally  beneath 
channel just upstream  of a 
prominent  knickpoint  eroded 
into till.  Both banks have 
recently been lined with 
boulder riprap, and large 
cobbles have been placed along 
the bed. 
I‐069 1  P  MH  Sharp meander exhibiting  
lateral erosion is encroaching  
on the manhole adjacent to the 
channel.  Its rate of migration  
has likely slowed due to recent 
bank stabilization  measures  
including re‐grading, boulder 
riprap placement  and willow 
planting.  

                                                                                                                                   
 Site 
ID Reach Type At‐risk 
Infrastructure  Description Photo
I‐151 1  I  Pipe  Pipe crosses diagonally  beneath 
locally sand‐embedded  channel 
bed at relatively straight 
section of channel, between a 
stormwater  outfall on the east 
bank and recent boulder 
placement  along the west bank. 
P‐068 1  P/I  MH/pipe  Meander has slowly migrated 
eastward  over the buried pipe.  
The rate of migration  has likely 
slowed since the eastern bank 
was lined with boulder riprap 
that transitions  downstream  
into an armourstone  wall 
protecting  an adjacent 
manhole.   Scouring along the 
toe of the riprap and wall have 
the potential to undermine  and 
destabilize  the revetment,  and 
reduce the depth of cover 
above the buried sewer pipe.  
P‐065 2  P/I  MH/pipe  A prominent  down valley‐
migrating  meander,  with slight 
lateral erosion that prompted  
cobble riprap placement  along 
the eastern bank to protect the 
adjacent buried sewer and 
manhole,  has recently been 
cut‐off naturally such that 
erosion is focused more along 
the western bank.  
P‐062 2  P/I  Pipe  A down valley‐migrating  
meander that encroached  on 
the buried sewer has slowed its 
rate of migration  due to recent 
construction  of a now‐
collapsing  crib wall along the 
eastern bank, and has begun to 
infill and aggrade. 
P‐
060a 2  P/I  Pipe  A scallop‐shaped hollow eroded 
into the eastern bank, 
presumably  due to flow 
deflection  around a former 
logjam, has encroached  on the 
buried sewer.  The hollow has 
become embedded  with sand 
on its bed and has begun to 
revegetate  its bank now that 
the thalweg is again centred 
within the channel.  

                                                                                                                                   
 Site 
ID Reach Type At‐risk 
Infrastructure  Description Photo
P‐
060b 2  P  MH  Gradual lateral and slight up‐
valley migration  of a meander 
has caused undercutting  and 
collapse of the eastern bank.  
Tension cracks have formed in 
the soil around an adjacent 
manhole.  
I‐152 2  I/P  Pipe/MH  Gradual down‐valley migration  
of a meander with an 
amplitude  nearly as large as the 
meander belt has undercut and 
destabilized  the bank 
immediately  adjacent to a 
manhole.   The channel bed 
over the sewer crossing is 
gradually infilling and 
aggrading,  increasing  the 
frequency  of overbank  flooding 
across the neck of the tortuous 
meander.   
I‐153 2  I  Pipe  Situated at the inflection  point 
between two meanders,  this 
site has undergone  minor 
undercutting  and slumping of 
its eastern bank and deposition  
along the western bank, 
resulting in slight infilling and 
aggradation  overall. 
I‐154 3  I  Pipe  At the downstream  limit of 
historically  straightened  
channel, this site no longer 
exhibits evidence of 
degradation,  with a bed partly 
embedded  in sand, and exhibits 
only minor bank erosion. 
P‐508 3  P/I  Pipe  A scallop‐shaped hollow eroded 
into the western bank, 
presumably  due to flow 
deflection  from the historical 
placement  of concrete slabs 
along the eastern bank and 
possibly from a former logjam, 
has encroached  on the buried 
sewer.  The rate of westward  
scour appears to have slowed 
and large riffle has formed on 
the bed.  

                                                                                                                                   
 Site 
ID Reach Type At‐risk 
Infrastructure  Description Photo
I‐155 3  I  Pipe  A shallow pool has formed at 
this site that is located at the 
downstream  end of a slight 
bend in the historically  
straightened  channel.  Both 
banks exhibit erosion. 
P‐058 3  P  MH  Within this historically  
straightened  section of 
channel, a gradual increase in 
thalweg sinuosity has initiated 
erosion along the eastern bank, 
which is composed  of fill 
overlying alluvium. 
P‐057 3  P  MH  A gradual increase in thalweg 
sinuosity,  in phase with a slight 
bend in this section of 
historically  straightened  
channel, has resulted in severe 
erosion along the eastern bank 
leaving little fill and alluvial 
material between the adjacent 
manhole.   
P‐531 3  P  MH  A gradual increase in thalweg 
sinuosity,  exacerbated  by in‐
stream logjams and the in‐
phase bend immediately  
upstream,  has resulted in 
severe erosion along the high, 
steep eastern bank and the 
partial exposure of an adjacent 
manhole in the bank.

 LER Index = 3*0.15 + 3*0.15 + 3*0.10 + 2*0.10 + 3*0.075 + 3*0. 50 + 3*0.075 + 3*0.20 + 3*0.10 
= 2.90 
 This LER Index of 2.90 for Site P‐531 represents  the highest‐ranked site with respect to lateral 
erosion risk in the study area.  This is consistent  
with TRCA’s “high” ranking for site P‐531 in its 
RFP for this project, and its prioritization  of this site for the design of stabilization  measures  
through a subsequent  RFP.  Severe bank erosion along the outer bank of a slight meander,  
exacerbated  by flow deflection  around large woody debris, has led to the partial exposure of a 
manhole high above the channel bed.  The failing bank is composed  of silty to sandy fill material 
overlying alluvium of similar texture, and recent sloughs have stripped virtually all vegetation  
from its surface.

VER Index = 3*0.20 + 3*0.50 + 1*0.30 = 2.40 
 
This VER Index of 2.40 represents  the second highest‐ranked site with respect to vertical 
erosion risk in the study area.  This ranking is consistent  with, or possibly may be considered  
slightly higher than, TRCA’s “medium”  priority for remediation  identified  in its RFP for this 
project.  Both the reach‐ and site‐scale processes  reveal a bed‐lowering trend, in this case 
primarily through upstream  knickpoint  migration.   TRCA’s survey documented  a depth of cover 
of approximately  1.4 m in 2012, but the continued  erosion of the knickpoint  at the head of the 
~1 m‐deep scour pool at its base may continue to reduce this separation.

Current Status: Design and permits for P-531 Emergency Maintenance Hole Rehabil itation is complete 
and implementation is scheduled for July 2017. Greck and Associ ates has been 
retained to complete detailed designs at Site 1 (P-057) and Sit e 4 (P-070/I-149), as well 
as concept designs for Site 2 (P-060/I-152) and Site 3 P-068 un der Schedule A+ 
MCEAs in 2017. Implementation at Site 1 is to be completed afte r site P-531 in late 
August/September 2017. Implementation at P-057 is contingent upon receipt of 
CWWF funding.  
     
BACKGROUND 
The Infrastructure Hazard Monitoring Program identified risks t o multiple sanitary sites between John Street 
and Steeles Ave in the City of Markham. Of all of the identifie d sites 6 were ranked high priority: I-152, P-057, 
P-060, P-068, P-070, & P-531. Due to the associated risk to mun icipal infrastructure, TRCA decided to 
undertake a geomorphic assessment of a 1250 linear metre stretc h of German Mills Creek, which was 
received from Greck & Associat es / Palmer Environmental Consult ing Group in October 2015. This 
assessment identified another high priority site, I-149, which is going to be addressed in combination with P-
070 due to their close proximity to one another.  
Site P-531 is currently the highest priority in the Region requ iring emergency remedial action and will undergo 
remedial works in July 2017.  Utilizing the geomorphic assessme nt, TRCA Engineering Projects created the 
detailed design for emergency temporary maintenance hole stabil ization at Site P-531 in house. The 
emergency remedial actions at Site P-531 will be conducted as a  Schedule A+ Municipal Class Environmental 
Assessment.   
Additionally, the Region requested that deteriorated components  of maintenance hole LC-16 be replaced as a 
part of the remedial works at site P-531. As a result, Greck an d Associates Ltd. was retained on a competitive 
basis and completed the maintenance rehabilitation design in De cember of 2016. 
 The remedial bank stabilization and sanitary protection works a t site P-531 includes a Filtrexx Severe Slope 
Stabilization treatment for the upper portion of the bank above  the 2-year waterline. This work has been 
contracted out as a design-build aspect of the project in Janua ry 2017 and will be installed following the 
installation of the vegetated buttress and maintenance hole rep airs. 
 Greck and Associates has been retained to complete detailed des igns at Site 1 (P-057) and Site 4 (P-070/I-
149), as well as concept designs for Site 2 (P-060/I-152) and S ite 3 P-068 under Schedule A+ MCEAs in 2017. 
Implementation at Site 1 is to be completed in summer 2017.

PROJECT NAME :  German Mi lls Settlers ( Berczy Wycliffe ) Park Sanitary  Infrastructure Protection Project  
Municipality:  Markham  / York Region  CFN:  50675  
Account code : 189-26  2018  Budget:  $525,987.00  
Funding source:  York Region CWW F, 189 -26 (2018 Cost Recoverable)  
Current Status:  • Site 1 (P -057) implementation  January - March 2018  
• Draft detailed designs have been received from Greck for Site 4 and conceptual 
designs have been received for Sites 2 & 3  
• Sites 2 & 3 planning and conceptual designs will proceed under the Schedule B 
MCEA process , including PIC meetings  in 2018  
• Site 4 is scheduled for implementation in 2019 under a Schedule A+ MCEA 
process  
    
BACKGROUND 
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) is working in partnership with York Region to undertake 
sanitary infrastructure protection works at multiple sites within German Mills Settlers Park.  Since 2012, the 
TRCA Infrastructur e Hazard Monitoring Program (IHMP) has identified and monitored several at -risk sanitary 
sites along German Mills Creek, a tributary of the East Don River, in German Mills Settlers Park. A total of seven sites have been ranked in terms of priority and are in the planning stages of related sanitary 
infrastructure protection works. Greck and Associates Ltd. was retained to complete detailed designs at Site 1 
(P-057), Site 2 (P -060/I -152), Site 3 P -068 and Site 4 (P -070/I -149) under Schedule A+ MCEAs in 2017.  
 
Documented monitoring visits up to and including the 2017 annual inspection have classified Site 1 (P-057) as 
a high priority site. Greck’s design for remedial bank stabilization works include a 110 m round stone 
revetment , two woody debris treatments, three rock vanes, and a 20  m section of armour stone wall to protect 
the channel bank immediately surrounding sanitary maintenance hole LC -14 at Site 1 . Update: As of February 
21, 2018, Greck and Associates have revised the Site 1 design to replace the arm ourstone wall with a boulder 
revetment and top of bank MSE structure following poor soil conditions and ground water upwelling during 
construction.  
The draft design concepts have been received for Sites 2 and 3 (P -060/I -152 and P -068) and offered  localized  
and major channel realignment options. The next S ite 2 & 3 draft concept design package , with an evaluation 
report and EIS , will be completed after the PIC meeting  that is scheduled for late spring 2018.  TRCA will 
proceed through the Schedule B MCEA planning process in 2018 with implementation of Sites 2 & 3 works anticipated for summer 2020.  
Currently, Site 4 is recognized as a high priority site requiring remedial action and has been monitored by TRCA since 2011. It contains a proximity (P -070) and intersecti on site (I -149) to be addressed . As of the most 
recent inspection at the intersection site, f ailed gabion baskets are deposited in the channel and on the right 
bank in the area of the crossing. The su rveyed depth of cover is 0.80 m and 0.58 m above sanitary 
infrastructure . The live cribwall is failing with logs at the midsection broken and displaced . The bank behind the 
cribwall has a scour more than 50m length.  Draft detailed designs have been received from Greck for Site 4. 
TRCA will finalize and obtain all permits and approvals under a Schedule A+ MCEA process in 2018. Site 4  
implementation is scheduled for 2019, pending available resources . 
 Federal, provincial, and municipal funding for this project has been received through the Clean Water and 
Wastewater Fund (CWWF)  for implementation at Site 1 and studies at Sites 2 – 4. Th e grant contributes  a total 
of $450,000.00  and deliverables must be completed by March 31, 2020. Cost tracking for these works is being 
documented under account 189 -26B. 
 
    
2 of 7 SCOPE OF WORK  
The proposed scope of work for 2018 is to:  
• Complete bank stabilization works at Site 1 , including  
o A 110 m round st one buttress  
o Two woody debris habitat treatments  
o Three rock vanes  
o A roundstone revetment with a vertical MSE at sanitary maintenance hole LC -14  
• Finalize detailed designs for Site 4, obtain all necessary permits and approvals to allow for 
implementation in 2019  as a Schedule A+ MCEA  
• Finalize conceptual designs for Sites 2 & 3, and obtain all necessary permits and approvals under a  
Schedule B MCEA  
 
PROJECT LOCATION  
The sites are located  along a 1400 m stretch of German Mills Creek  running north- south  between John Street  
and Steeles Avenue East , west of Don Mills Road .

Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    ii 
 Executive Summary  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) continues to work toward safeguarding people and property from the 
adverse impacts of flooding and other natural hazards and enhancing the health of watershed communities. Through 
programs that balance human, environmental, and economic needs, TRCA promote s healthy rivers and shorelines, 
protection of greenspaces and biodiversity, and sustainable and safe communities. TRCA’s Erosion Risk Management 
Program (ERMP) is one such initiative established to encourage proactive prevention, protection, and managemen t of 
erosion hazards on private and public property. The proposed works outlined in this Project Plan are remedial erosion control  
works supported by the ERMP to provide long -term protection for life and property.  
The Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization P roject is being completed in accordance with the Class Environmental 
Assessment for Remedial Flood and Erosion Control Projects, amended 2013 (Class EA), with the objective of reducing risk 
to life and property, as per the mandate of Conservation Authoriti es under the Conservation Authorities Act (R.S.O 1990).  
Three primary objectives were identified for this project through the Class EA process:  
1. Risk Mitigation   To protect human life and property from the hazards of erosion;  
2. Naturalization   To support a native terrestrial ecosystem habitat;  
3. Compatibility   To ensure that the project is compatible with existing infrastructure and the surrounding 
environment.  
Concerns regarding slope instability behind Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto, wer e first identified to TRCA staff  
following the July 8, 2013 severe storm event. The Project limits incorporate  a segment of  valley  slope located behind three 
(3) residential properties  located at 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto . Following visual inspections in 
2013 by TRCA Erosion Risk Management  staff , this site was recommended to undergo a detailed slope stability and erosion 
risk assessment (SS&ERA) to  determine the type and extent of risk to essential structures at the top of slope . The SS&ERA 
was completed  by Cole Engineering Group Ltd.  in 2015 and identified o ne (1) private residential dwelling  to be  at long -term 
risk from upper slope instability should no works take place; t he other  two properties were identified to be at risk of loss  of 
tableland should no works take place.  Based on the results of this assessment, remedial works were recommended to 
provide long -term, low -maintenance protection to the properties at the top of slope  from the risk s of upper slope instability . 
The Class EA for this  Project was officially initiated on November 22, 2018 with the publication of a Notice of Intent in the 
North York Mirror. The Notice of Intent and subsequent official project update notices w ere also distributed to Indigenous 
communities, local community groups, homeowners with in the vicinity of the Project, and regulatory agencies including 
Conservation Ontario (CO), the Ministry of Environment, Conservation and Parks (MECP), the Ministry of Natural Resources 
and Forestry (MNRF), and the City of Toronto.  
As part of the Class EA process, a range of  slope stabilization solutions were developed and evaluated with input from a 
Community Liaison Committee (CLC). The CLC was comprised of technical staff, stakeholders, and interested members of 
the public.  The outcome of the Class EA planning process dete rmined that construction of a Mechanically Stabilized Earth 
(MSE) structure along the upper slope behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent was the preferred slope stabilization solution 
to be implemented.   
Following the thirty (30) day stakeholder and public review period of this Class EA report and the successful resolution of 
any concerns received during this review, TRCA intends to finalize the detailed design of  the MSE structure and obtain the 
necessary approvals to proceed with its implementation.

Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    1 
 1.0 I NTRODUCTION  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) has proposed  to undertake  remedial slope stabilization works 
to address upper slope instability along a valley slope behind three (3) private residential properties located at 
21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto.  The project site is located northeast of Jane Street and 
Wilson Avenue within the Humber River Waters hed (Figure 1 ). TRCA was notified of the slope failure behind 
Peacham Crescent following the July 8, 2013 severe weather event.  
This Project is being completed in accordance with the Class Environmental Assessment for Remedial Flood and 
Erosion Control Pr ojects (Class EA) (amended 2013). The purpose of the Peacham  Crescent Slope Stabilization 
Project (hereafter referred to as “the Project”), is to take action to protect human life and property from the 
hazards of valley slope erosion by providing long -term , low maintenance protection for the three (3) private 
properties within the Project limits.  
This Projec t Plan has been prepared as documentation of the decision -making process exercised when selecting 
the preferred measure(s) for carrying out the propose d remedial work. This Project Plan  includes:  
• the situation or problem to be addressed, including the cause(s) and history of the problem;  
• the preparation of a baseline inventory to provide the information needed to evaluate the alternative 
measures;  
• the e xamination of a full range of alternative remedial measures and the selection of a preferred alternative. 
Advantages and disadvantages of each alternative are considered in the analysis, including a “Do Nothing” 
option;  
• the rationale underlying the selecti on of the preferred alternative method to carry out the remedial work;  
• an environmental analysis of the preferred alternative, including a detailed study of the temporary and 
permanent net impacts of the proposed project;  
• identification of methods for avoiding or mitigating negative impacts;  
• a record of consultation with interested persons, i ndigenous communities, government agencies, and 
community groups;  
• an outline of the monitoring program which will commence upon completion of the work.

 1.1 Relationship of the Undertaking to the Environmental Assessment Act  
TRCA is defined as a public body in Sect ion 3 of Regulation 334/90 in the Environmental Assessment Act (R.S.O. 
1990)  (EA Act) and must conduct its remedial flood and erosion control projects in accordance with said Act.  
Recognizing that common elements exist in addressing flood and erosion probl ems, a coordinated approach to 
environmental assessments was developed by Conservation Ontario (CO) in 1993 for use by all Conservation 
Authorities (CAs) , referred to as the Class Environmental Assessment for Remedial Flood and Erosion Control 
Projects (Class EA)  (amended 2013) . This project was planned and will be completed in accordance with the Class 
EA process and aligns with the following excerpt from the CO Class EA document:  
“Remedial Flood and Erosion Control Projects refer to those Projects undertaken by Conservation Authorities, 
which are required to protect human life and property, in previously developed areas, from an impending flood 
or erosion problem. Such Projects do not include works which facilitate or anticipate development. Major f lood 
and erosion control undertakings which do not suit this definition, such as multipurpose Projects, lie outside the 
limits of this Class and require an Individual Environmental Assessment”  (CO, 2013).  
Over twenty years of experience have demonstrated that using the Class EA approach is an effective way of 
complying with the EA Act’s requirements. Approval of the Class EA allows CAs to carry out these types of projects 
without applying for formal approval under the EA Act on the condition that all other necessary federal and 
provincial approvals are obtained (CO, 2013). A chart illustrating the key steps of the Class EA planning and design 
process is shown i n Figure 2.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    4

 1.2 Purpose of the Undertaking  
A. Risk Mitigation  
A slope failure behind three (3)  properties located at 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto, 
was first identified to TRCA following the July 8, 2013 severe weather event. After visual inspections by TRCA 
Erosion  Risk Management  staff, it was noted that a failure  had occurred along the upper slope causing damage 
to an existing wooden retaining wall, causing loss of the tableland behind these three properties. TRCA 
subsequ ently retained Cole Engineering  Group Ltd. , an engineering consultant , to conduct a slope stability and 
erosion risk assessment (SS&ERA) . The purpose of the SS&ERA was  to determine  the extent of risk to essential 
structures at this site. The SS&ERA was completed in 2015 and identified that one (1) private residential dwelling 
is at-risk should no remedial control works be undertaken.  
Based on the results of the SS&ERA, remedial slope stabilization measures were recommended to be undertaken 
behind Peacham Crescent. The m ain objective of the Project is to safeguard human life and essential structures 
within the Project limits . Essential structures and property will be protected from the  hazards  of slope instability 
through the implementation of a n effective long -term, low maintenance slope stabilization solution.  
In addition to being planned in accordance with the Class EA process, the Project will also be carried out in 
accordance with The Living City Policies for Planning and Development in the Watersheds of the Toronto and 
Region Conservation Authority  (TRCA, 2014), which seeks to reduce and eliminate existing flood and erosion 
hazards to rehabilitate valley and stream corridors on private and public lands ( Appendix A ).  
B. Naturalization  
The second objective of the Project is to implement a solution that will not only result in  upper slope stabilization 
but will also strive to  improve the natural ravine habitat behind  Peacham Crescent. The  identified preferred 
solution for slope stabilization, in conjunction with post -construction site restoration , are based on the 
incorporat ion of  native plant species that will:  
• Provide increased stabilization to the slope face  
• Increase resiliency to erosio n forces resulting from rain and runoff  
• Enhance biodiversity of terrestrial habitat species  
• Improve the ecological health of the surrounding community by providing better resiliency to future changes 
in the environment  
C. Compatibility  
The third objective recognizes that there are existing essential structures and infrastructure adjacent to the 
Project l imits which must be considered when developing remedial design options to ensure compatibility 
between proposed works and the built environment. Achieving c ompatibility has been accomplished through 
the evaluation of a range of conceptual design solutions . The evaluation of conceptual solutions resulted in the 
identification  of a preferred solution that best integrates key design elements necessary to provide  protection 
from the hazards of slope instability  with existing physical, biological, and social environment s surrounding the 
Project site.

1.4 General Description of the Undertaking  
There are four situations in which remedi al flood and erosion control projects may be undertaken within the 
Class EA framework:  
1. Riverine flooding  
2. Riverine and valley slope erosion  
3. Shoreline flooding  
4. Shoreline erosion  
The slope instability resulting from the July 8, 2013 severe storm event behind the affected properties on  
Peacham Crescent is classified as  riverine and valley slope erosion. However, the slope stabilization works being 

Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    9 
 planned for this project will be isolated to the upper valley slope at the rear of these three properties and will 
not involve any riverine works.  
In accordance with the Class EA planning process, a full range of alternatives must be developed, including both 
traditional and innovative approaches. The type and range of alternatives developed will vary by project as the y 
are based on the nature, cause and extent of the problem. The options developed must be tailored to the 
individual characteristics of each site.  
The Project examined (3) three  remedial alternative solutions to achieve the objectives:  
Alternative 1  – ‘Do Nothing’  
Alternative 2  – Regrade and revegetate  
Alternative 3 –  Mechanically Stabilized Earth (MSE) Structure  
In determining the preferred method of remediation for the erosion risk  and slope instability problem,  two major 
factors were considered: risk to structure(s) and the cause(s) of the hazard. According to TRCA ’s design criteria, 
the potential risk to existing structures is the most important factor and subsequently given greater weight than 
the physical and geological condition associated with the cause of erosion.  During evaluation of the  preliminary 
alternatives, preference was also given to alternatives with a smaller construction footprint and machin e access 
requirements to reduce impacts to the natural and built environment.  
In all cases, the design of slope stability and erosion control works  must provide protection compatible with 
TRCA’s design criteria, which includes improvements to , or enhanc ements of , the existing terrestrial and aquatic 
habitat conditions through natural designs. Upon review, the proposed undertaking meets all TRCA planning and 
policy objectives  and satisfies the needs and concerns of the affected property owners and general  public based 
on the input received during the Community Liaison Committee (CLC) meeting  and outreach activities.  
The decision -making process used in the selection of the preferred remedial solution is documented in detail in 
Section 4.0 . 
A record of consult ation activities, including copies of all CLC materials, can be found in Appendix D.  
1.5 Rationale for the Undertaking  
As part of its primary objective to prevent, eliminate, or reduce the risk of life and property from flooding and 
erosion, TRCA has  an Erosion Risk Management Program (ERMP) which monitors erosion and slope instability 
within TRCA’s jurisdiction. The ERMP includes monitoring private residential properties, at the request of 
landowners, and assessing risks to public property or infrast ructure. One component of the program is to monitor 
the rate of erosion at a site in relation to the position of permanent structures (e.g. houses, infrastructure). Signs 
of accelerated erosion may prompt further study through a formal investigation by a g eotechnical engineer.  
TRCA plans and implements major maintenance and remedial flood and erosion control work s on a priority basis 
due to the limit of available funding each year.  Prioritization  is based on the results from annual monitoring 
through the E RMP . Out of the hundreds of properties with reported damage following the July 8, 2013 severe 
storm event, 21, 23, and 25  Peacham Crescent were  prioritized for remedial work based on  a variety of factors:  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    10 
 • The location of the projected long- term stable slop e crest ( LTSSC ) in relation to permanent and essential 
structures at the top of slope;  
• The presence of a public footpath at the base of slope; and  
• The presence of potentially impacted infrastructure located within the tributary and surrounding area  
The LTS SC models the natural regression of a slope crest resulting from erosion forces and is based on the 
composition and conditions  of substrate materials. As part of the 2015 SS&ERA, Cole Engineering Group Ltd.  
conducted a subsurface investigation to delineate the LTSSC behind Peacham Crescent using a factor of safety 
(FoS) of 1.5. As can be seen in Figure 7, the positioning of th is LTSSC intersects the corner of the residential 
dwelling at 21 Peacham Crescent  indicating  that this dwelling  is at risk from the hazards of erosion and slope 
instability should no remedial slope stabilization works take place.  
In 2018 TRCA retained Terraprobe Inc. to develop conceptual solutions to remediate the erosion and  slope 
instability behind Peacham Crescent using the substrate  data previously collected  for Cole Engineering Group 
Ltd.’s SS&ERA.  
Prior to deciding to proceed with a Class EA and plan for remedial action at this site, TRCA evaluated the ‘ Do 
Nothing’ option, which assessed what would happen if no remedial works were implemented  at the Project site. 
The detailed slope stability and erosion risk assessment undertaken by Cole Engineering Group Ltd.in 2015 
indicated a higher likelihood for a substantial loss of tableland, landscaping structures, and ongoing risk to life 
and property should no remedial works be implemented.   
Considering the potential risks if a ‘Do Nothing’ approach was taken , TRC A made the decision to proceed with a 
Class EA.  Upon approval of this Class EA Project Plan, it is intended that work will commence immediately to 
implement the preferred alternative solution to provide erosion control and slope stabilization to the upper slope 
behind Peacham Crescent.

 2.0 BACKGROUND  
This section provides an overview of the causes and extent of erosion and slope instability hazards 
impacting the Project site and includes justification for TRCA’s involvement and approach to developing a 
remedial solution. The studies and assessments und ertaken by TRCA to evaluate the Project site are listed 
herein and discussed in further detail under Section 3.0 –  Baseline Inventory . 
2.1 Justification of Authority Involvement  
TRCA has a primary goal to carry out remedial erosion control works as set out  in Chapter 27, Section 20  
of the Conservation Authorities Act (R.S.O. 1990):  
“The objects of an authority are to establish and undertake, in the area which it has jurisdiction, a 
program designed to further the conservation, restoration, development and management of 
natural resources other than gas, oil, coal and minerals (R.S.O. 1990, C.27, s.20).”  
As part of this goal, CAs are considered to have prime responsibility over water management in terms of 
water quantity and related hazards through administrative and regulatory powers. In the 1980 Watershed 
Plan, TRCA developed and implemented its Erosion and Sediment Control Program (ESCP) with two major 
directions:  
“To minimize the aggravation or creation of erosion or sediment prob lems as a result of new 
development, and to rectify existing problems through protective works” (TRCA, 1980).  
These directions are categorized as either preventative or protective, respectively. The Project falls under 
the protection component of the ESCP,  which is designed to protect lives and minimize loss of property 
through the construction of suitable remedial works. Through annual capital funding from its member 
municipalities, TRCA is able to implement a program of major remedial works for slope stab ilization 
throughout the watersheds of the Greater Toronto Area.  
2.2 History of the Problem  
On the evening of July 8, 2013, a severe storm event in the Greater Toronto Area resulted in widespread 
flooding, erosion, and slope failures in the west end and do wntown areas of the City of Toronto. Flash 
flooding and accelerated soil erosion resulted in extensive damage to river and valley systems and put 
many residential properties at risk. City of Toronto water and wastewater infrastructure and many park 
ameniti es also suffered damage due to the extensive flooding. In the days following the storm, TRCA 
responded to more than 450 reports of damage to private property that ranged from minor bank erosion 
and collapsing retaining walls to major landslides.  
Based on v isual inspections by TRCA staff, additional subsurface investigations were recommended for 
approximately 150 properties with the most severe damage to determine the extent of risk to existing 
structures. The purpose of the geotechnical assessments was to a ssist TRCA to further rank the hundreds 
of private properties affected by the July 8, 2013 severe storm event based on a variety of factors including 
risk to human life, property, and essential structures. TRCA retained Cole Engineering Group Ltd. in 2015 
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    13 
 to complete a subsurface investigation at Peacham Crescent to determine the extent of risk from the 
hazards of erosion and slope instability ( Appendix A ). 
2.3 Planning Documents  
In developing the range of alternative solutions for evaluation under the Clas s EA guidelines, TRCA 
incorporated the applicable guidelines from several municipal, provincial, and federal guidance and policy 
documents, as follows:  
Valley and Stream Corridor Management Program (1994)  
The Valley and Stream Corridor Management Program ( VSCMP) is a guidance document developed by 
TRCA to direct land use activities and development within valley and st ream corridors. The program 
acknowledges the need for risk management related to flooding, erosion, and slope instability, while 
ensuring that  future policies and criteria govern any change to existing resource -based uses of valley and 
stream corridors. The program also offers recommendations for the rehabilitation of valley and stream 
corridors that help to direct short and long- term resource p lanning activities.  
Toronto and Region Remedial Action Plan (1994)  
The Toronto and Region Remedial Action Plan (RAP) was developed by all levels of government and other 
stakeholders. The RAP encompasses a 2,000 km2 area within TRCA’s jurisdiction in the City of Toronto. 
This area includes the Lake Ontario waterfront and the watersheds between Etobicoke Creek in the west 
and Rouge River in the east. The Toronto RAP is managed by representatives from Environment and 
Climate Change Canada, Ontario Ministry of the Environmen t, Conservation and Parks , MNRF, City of 
Toronto, and TRCA. The RAP has been working towards the following goals:  
• Clean waters  
• Healthy habitats  
• Science and monitoring  
• Sustainability  
• Education and involvement  
Terrestrial Natural Heritage Syst em Strategy (2006)  
TRCA’s Terrestrial Natural Heritage System Strategy (TNHSS) was designed to enhance biodiversity and 
quality of life for residents by increasing the amount of forest and wetland habitats. It uses a science -
based analytical tool based on ecological criteria to identify an expanded and targeted land base for 
inclusion in a terrestrial natural heritage system. TNHSS was designed for the entire TRCA jurisdiction as 
terrestrial systems and their interactions span watershed boundaries. The target system relates t o the 
terrestrial component of the natural heritage system. TNHSS contains a number of strategic directions 
including proposed land use planning policies, land management, stewardship and education 
opportunities, and long- term monitoring.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    14 
 Planning and Development Procedural Manual (2007)  
TRCA’s Planning and Development Procedural Manual provides a comprehensive summary of the 
legislative and policy framework that guides TRCA’s decisions and actions with respect to planning and 
regulatory responsibilitie s, as outlined in Ontario Regulation 166/06.  
The Living City Policies for Planning and Development in the Watersheds of the Toronto and Region 
Conservation Authority (2014)  
The Living City Policies for Planning and Development in the Watersheds of the Toro nto and Region 
Conservation Authority (LCP) is a CA policy document that guides the implementation of TRCA’s legislated 
and delegated roles and responsibilities in the planning and development approvals process. Comparable 
to a combined municipal official plan and zoning bylaw, the LCP represents a compilation of existing plan 
and permit review policies and practices that have evolved over time. It also contains new policies related 
to TRCA programs, scientific research, and external planning and developmen t initiatives.  
TRCA’s main program previously guiding the Authority in its planning and regulatory roles was the VSCMP 
endorsed by the Authority in October 1994. The LCP supersedes the VSCMP while continuing and 
expanding on the VSCMP’s valuable foundatio n of principles and policy intent.  
2.4 Supporting Studies and Assessments  
2.2.1 Physical Studies  
A) TRCA Contract RSD 14 -03 Storm Damage Detailed Slope Stability and Erosion Risk Assessment – 
Area E Properties, City of Toronto (Cole Engineering Group Ltd.,  2015)  
In 2015, a geotechnical investigation was completed by Cole Engineering Group Ltd. to determine the 
position of the LTSSC along a section of valley slope behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent at a factor 
of safety of 1.5. Cole Engineering Group Ltd . worked with Soil Engineers Ltd. to advance five (5) boreholes, 
one of which was used as a monitoring well to assess ground water conditions, to determine the 
composition and existing conditions of subsurface materials and provide recommendations from a 
geotechnical perspective.  
B) Conceptual Alternatives Report for Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization behind 21, 23, & 25 
Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto (Terraprobe Inc., 2019)  
In 2018, Terraprobe Inc. was retained to interpret the subsurface da ta collected by Cole Engineering 
Group Ltd.  (2014)  to remodel the projected LTSSC using a factor of safety of 1.3 and subsequently develop 
three (3) conceptual alternative solutions, in addition to the ‘Do Nothing’ alternative, to address the 
erosion and s lope instability behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent.  
For a full account of the Physical condition studies of the Project area please refer to Appendix A for the 
complete geotechnical report(s).

 2.2.2 Biological Studies  
A)  Ecology Survey (TRCA, 2018a)  
A terrestrial biological survey of the natural area within and surrounding the Project area was undertaken 
by TRCA’s Environmental Monitoring and Data Management (EMDM) to inventory existing flora, fauna, 
and vegetation communities within the study area. T his data was gathered to inform planning and design 
activities and to ensure decisions are made with consideration of the local ecosystem.  
B)  Butternut (Juglans cinerea) Genetic Testing (Ontario Forest Research Institute, 2019)  
Butternuts are an endangere d tree species within North America and are protected under Ontario’s 
Endangered Species Act. Genetic testing of dormant bud tissue was conducted on an identified Butternut 
located within the limits of a proposed construction access route to the Project si te. The testing was done 
to determine its purity and subsequently inform decisions related to its protection.  
For a full account of the biological studies of the Project area please refer to Appendix C for the complete 
biological survey report(s).  
2.2.3 Cultural Studies  
A)  Archaeological Assessment (Stage 1 & 2) in the City of Toronto, 21 -25 Peacham Crescent Slope 
Stabilization, Lots 12 and 13, Concession IV, Geographic Township of York, Historic York County 
(TRCA, 2017)  
As required under the Ontario Heritage Act, R.S.O 1990, the Project site was evaluated for the potential 
to encounter archaeological resources th at could be affected by the proposed slope stabilization works. 
The Stage 1 assessment was followed by a Stage 2 assessment, which involved a field investigation to 
attempt to locate potential artifacts and confirm presence or absence of an archaeological site. No cultural 
resources were identified during the Stage 2 assessment, therefore no further archaeological assessment 
was recommended.  
B)  Stage 1 Archaeological Assessment, 21 -25 Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization, Lot 13, 
Concession IV West of Yong e Street, Geographic Township of York, Historic York County in the City 
of Toronto (TRCA, 2018b)  
A secondary Stage 1 Archaeological Assessment was conducted to evaluate archaeological potential for 
an expanded study area surrounding the Project limits to i nclude the full extent of the construction work, 
access, and staging areas. Areas identified as retaining archaeological potential were recommended for 
further Stage 2 archaeological assessment prior to any ground disturbing works.  
For a full account of t he socioeconomic and cultural history of the Project area please refer to Appendix 
A for the complete archaeological assessment report(s).

 3.0 BASELINE ENVIRONMENT AL INVENTORY  
Once it has been determined t hat remedial works are warranted at a given site, a baseline inventory is 
prepared. The baseline inventory provides the information needed to evaluate the alternative options 
developed through the Class EA process, and to evaluate the types and level of environmental impacts 
that may result from imp lementing the preferred alternative.  
The inventory involves the examination and documentation of:  
• the erosion problem;  
• existing site conditions, including physical, biological, cultural and socioeconomic characteristics;  
• engineering/technical aspects to be  considered;  
• previous protective measures that have been implemented within the Project limits.  
• whether the site falls within a vulnerable area as identified in the local assessment report prepared 
under the Clean Water Act , 2006  
This baseline environmental inventory takes into consideration the directly and indirectly affected 
environment. The area indirectly affected by the Project includes Exbury Park  behind Peacham Crescent 
that connects Heathrow Park to Giovanni Caboto Park . Users of the paved pathway  that runs through this 
corridor will be temporarily impeded by the location of the machine acces s route proposed for site access. 
The private properties adjacent to the Project site,  in addition to those on the other side of the valley 
directly across from the Project site, may experience minor noise disturbances from the use of large 
machinery. Howe ver, all work will comply with the City of Toronto’s Noise Bylaw.  
Baseline environmental data was collected from the following organizations due to their specific expertise 
relevant to the regional and local Project area:   
• City of Toronto ; 
• TRCA ; 
• Ontario Ministry of Natural Resources and Forestry  (MNRF) ; 
• Ontario Ministry of the Environment, Conservation and Parks  (MECP)  
• Environment Canada  
3.1 Existing Site Conditions  
In accordance with the Class EA process, the broad definition of ‘environment’ as provided in the  
Environmental Assessment Act is applied to this section. The prepared environmental description is “an 
inventory of elements for which a given project is likely to have an impact”  (CO, 2013). The inventory 
includes an evaluation of the pres ence and extent of physical, biological, cultural, social, economic, and 
technical engineering elements applicable to the Project limits.  
An existing condition  drawing of the Project limits is included in Appendix  B.

 3.1.1 Physical Environment 
Unique Landf orms  
There are no known unique landforms within  or adjacent to  the Project limits ; therefore, there will be no 
impact s resulting  from the proposed works.  
Existing Mineral/Aggregate Resource Extraction Industries  
There are no existing mineral/aggregate resource extraction industries within or adjacent to  the Project 
limits; therefore, there will be no impact s resulting from the proposed works.  
Earth Science –  Areas of Natural and Scientific Interest (ANSI)  
There are no existing earth science areas of natural and scientific interest (ANSI) within or adjacent to the 
Project limits; therefore, there will be no impact s resulting from the proposed works.  
Specialty Crop Area /Agricultural Lands or Production  
There are no specialty crop s or agricultural lands or production areas within or adjacent to the Project 
limits; therefore, there will be no impact s resulting from the proposed works.  
Niagara Escarpment/Oak Ridges Moraine  
The Project limits do not fall within the boundaries of the Niagara Escarpment or the Oak Ridges Moraine; 
therefore, there will be no impact s resulting  from the proposed works.  
Environmentally Significant Areas (ESA) – Physical  
There are no physical environmentally significant areas (ESA) within or adjacent to the Project limits; 
therefore, there will be no impact s resulting  from the proposed works.  
Air Quality  
At a local scale, no significant sources of air pollution exist within or immediately surrounding the Project 
limits. No component of the Project is anticipated to significantly degrade air quality or be influenced by 
local or regional sources of air pollution. Any impacts from machinery and/or vehicles used as part of the 
construction phase will be temporary and minimal and ; therefor e, deemed insignificant.  
Agricultural Tile or Surface Drains  
There are no known agricultural tiles or surface drains within or adjacent to the Project limits.  
Improvements to surface drainage will be made through the design resulting in minimal surface ero sion.  
Noise Levels and Vibration  
The Project area is primarily located adjacent to low to medium density residential areas and there are no 
current noteworthy sources of noise or vibration within the area. Most noise is typically associated with 
vehicular traffic along Jane Street, to the west. All construction activities will be monitored for vibration 
and conform  to the City of Toronto’s noise bylaw.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    18 
 Water Flow Regime (Baseflow conditions  and storm conditions)  
The Black Creek tributary channel behind Peacham Crescent is used as an outlet for stormwater runoff 
from the surrounding residential lands. Typically, this channel has low -flow conditions and only influxes 
during and shortly following storm events or seasonal melts. T here will be no impac ts to the water flow 
regime of this tributary channel resulting from the proposed works . The proposed works will result in 
improvements to drainage and dissipation of runoff within the Project limits thereby reducing impacts to 
water flow . 
Existing Surface  Drainage/Groundwater Seepage/Groundwater Recharge and Discharge Zones  
There is existing surface drainage in the front of 21, 23, and 25  Peacham Crescent; City of Toronto 
stormwater infrastructure is located along Peacham Crescent and collects the runoff from the front of 
these properties. The rear yards of 21, 23, and 25  Peacham Crescent consist predominantly of permeable  
surfaces (i.e. lawn, garden, small aggregate material, etc.) allowing overland runoff to naturally drain 
through  the tableland  and recha rge the water table below. There was no observable groundwater 
seepage at any of these properties, indicating that the existing tableland is not oversaturated. Any 
overland flow that does not drain through the surface  into the water table will flow down the slope and 
collect in the Black Creek tributary channel that runs at the bottom of slope behind Peacham Crescent.   
Littoral Drift/Other Coastal Processes  
The Project limits are not located within a coastal setting; therefore, there will be no impacts to littoral 
drift or other coas tal processes resulting from the proposed works.  
Water Quality  
There will be no impacts to water quality resulting from the proposed works  as there are no channel or 
in-water works involved.  
Soil/Fill Quality  
Based on the analysis of the borehole samples b y Soil Engineers Ltd , the Project site has a thick layer of 
fill underlying a 0.15 m layer of topsoil. The fill layer varies from 0.8 to 1.5 m in thickness , consists of very 
loose to compact/soft to stiff earth fill materials , and is located predominantly along the upper slope . In 
their 2019 conceptual alternatives report for the Project site, it is noted by Terraprobe  Inc. (2019) that the 
extent of fill is likely greater at the slope face due to the presence of retaining structures and the fact that 
the boreholes were advanced away from the slope face and further in on tableland. L ayers of native silty 
clay, sandy silt, sandy silt till, silty fine sand, and fine sand  were found underlying the layer of fill.  Ultimately, 
the extent of fill material at the top of slope within the Project site was a significant contributor to the 
slope failure that occurred behind Peacham Crescent following the July 8, 2013 major storm event. The 
thick layer of fill resulted in overloading of the upper slope  and undermined the  natural soil strength  along 
the upper slope.  
Contaminated Soils/Sediment/Seeps  
A soils analysis was undertaken  to test soils within the Project limits for contaminant concentrations and 
levels of toxicity . The results of the soil analysis determined that the soils present within the Project limits 
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    19 
 are non -hazardous  and are classified as Table 2 – Residential Parkland Institutional (RPI) material, as per 
Ontario’s soil, ground water, and sediment standards . 
Existing Transportation Routes  
The main transportation routes surrounding the Project limits are Jane Street to the west, Wilson Avenue 
to the south , and Sheppar d Avenue West to the north. Jane Street is the closest main transportation route 
and is just over 500 m etres  away ( driving distance ). Major arterial roads that service the Project site 
include Highway 400 and  Highway 401 , which are located to the west and to the south, respectively . There 
will be no major alterations or impacts to existing transportation routes servicing the Project site. However, 
minor and temporary impacts are anticipated resulting from the delivery of material, machinery and other 
constr uction related activities.  
Constructed Crossings  
There are no constructed crossings within the limits of the Project or the construction access route. There 
is a stormwater outfall located near the construction access route and a pedestrian bridge near  the 
southern limits of the Project area. However , the offset distances of these two constructed crossings are 
significant enough that there will be no impacts r esulting from the proposed works.  
Geomorphology  
The Project limits  consist strictly of terrestrial land  and, as such, the Project area is subject only to surficial 
erosion . Overland runoff is the primary cause of surficial erosion and results in the formation of rills, gullies, 
and oversaturation of soil, which can significantly undermine s oil strength and lead to slope failure. The 
proposed works aim to increase the resiliency to surficial erosion thereby providing protection to essential 
structures and human life within the Project area from the hazards of erosion and slope instability. Th ere 
is a Black Creek tributary that runs through the parkland corridor at the bottom of slope, however, the 
bank nearest the Project limits are protected by an existing gabion basket wall in addition to a vegetated 
land buffer . As such , toe erosion is not a consideration for this Projec t.   
3.1.2 Biologica l Environment  
An in-field  terrestrial biological survey was undertaken by TRCA ecologists in 2018  to identify existing flora, 
fauna, and vegetation communities  within and adjacent to the Project limits  and determine an existing 
baseline for biological conditions. A supplementary review of existing environmental and ecological data 
sourced from various  government databases  was also undertaken to support the observations from the 
2018 ecology survey and develop a more comprehensive understanding of baseline conditions at the 
Project site. The findings of this biological research are discussed in further detail below. The full 2018 
ecology survey report and supporting documentation is found in Appendix C . 
Wildlife Habitat  
The lower limits of the Project area and the adjacent parkland corridor offer significant urban wildlife 
habitat  as it is densely vegeta ted with many undergrowth and forest  communities . In order to permit 
access for heavy machinery and create a suitable workspace  for the construct ion of  the preferred slope 
stabilization solution, t he proposed works will result in  temporary but significant disturbance to  the 
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    20 
 existing natural habitat within the Project limits . However, in effort to minimize impacts, all construction 
activities  are planned to have the smallest construction footprint as possible and occur outside of key 
migration and breeding w indows  when habitat use is not as crucial. A comprehensive restoration plan will 
follow construction activities and  remediate  disturbance to existing habitat  through the replanting of 
suitable native species.  
Habitat Linkages or Corridors  
The Project site is located adjacent to a stretch of parkland that measure s over one-kilometer  in length. 
This parkland  is densely  vegetated  with predominantly native species and c onsists of  ample tree cover . 
The parkland is surrounded  solely by urbanized land  and thus offers important habitat  for wildlife species . 
Due to its length, quality of habitat , and extent of the surrounding urbanized space, th is parkland  is 
considered  an important habitat corridor within the local environment.  
Although there will be no table disturbance to the habitat within the Project limits resulting from the 
proposed works , there will be no impact to the overall parkland corridor system. The Project area 
comprises a small portion of the total corridor area and will not fragment the length of corridor space. As 
previously noted, habitat d isturbance within the Project limits  will be mitigated through a comprehensive 
restoration plan , which will focus on rebuilding the local habitat  through the planting of suitable native 
species and ult imately help to create a more robust local environment . 
Significant Vegetation Communities  
There are twelve different types of vegetation communities that exist within, or extend into, the Project 
limits . Majority of these communities are various types of forests.  Of these 12 communities, six (6 ) are  
considered  communities of urban concern. As part of the implementation phase for these proposed works, 
selective tree and shrub clearing will be required  within the Project limits and along the constru ction 
access route  to allow for the operation of heavy machinery. However, the extent of tree and shrub 
removal will be minimal and highly selective; therefore, there should be no overall impact to the welfare 
of vegetation communities within the parkland corridor behind Peacham Crescent resulting from the 
proposed works.  
Environmentally Significant Areas (ESA) – Biological  
There are no Environmentally Significant Areas (ESA) within or adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, 
there will be no impact from  the proposed works.  
Fish Habitat  
A Black Creek tributary runs through the parkland corridor behind Peacham Crescent; however, the 2018 
ecology survey identified the tributary channel to be a turbid open aquatic environment (disturbed and 
unvegetated). These aquatic conditions are not suitable for fish habitat ion; therefore , there will be no 
impact to fish habitat s resulting from  the proposed works.   
Species of Concern -  Flora 
A butternut tree was identified within the limits of the proposed construction access route to the Project 
site. Due to the impacts of the disease known as butternut canker has had on butternut populations across 
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    21 
 North America, butternuts are classified as an  endangered species and are protected under Ontario’s 
Endangered Species Act. The results of a genetic test of dormant bud tissue determined that the butternut 
within the construction access route is genetically pure and therefore highly susceptible to but ternut 
canker .  
There were four tree species and one grass species identified within the Project site  that are classified as 
of urban concern. They are as follows : bitternut hickory ( Carya cordiformis ), American beech ( Fagus 
grandifolia), white grass ( Leersia virginica), red oak (Quercus rubra), and white pine ( Pinus strobus ). 
Although the removal of certain t rees and shrubs will be necessary to accommodate the operation of 
heavy machinery within the Project limits, the impacts resulting from the propos ed works on the  
aforementioned  species of  concern will be minimal; t ree or shrub removals will be planned and highly 
selective. Additionally, post -construction r estoration activities will focus on replanting suitable native 
species to compensate for those that were removed  and enhance the overall biodiversity of the Project 
site. Where possible, protection zones will also be erected during construction to protect sensitive native 
trees and shrubs within the vicinity of the worksite from undue harm.  
Species of Concern -  Fauna 
Dekay’s brownsnake ( Storeria dekayi ), red-eyed vireo ( Vireo olivaceus ), and white -breasted nuthatch 
(Sitta carolinensis ) were observed species of urban concern in the 2018 ecology survey. Although these 
species were not observed within the Project limits, there is potential for the se species to be affected by 
temporary  habita t destruction resulting from the implementation of these proposed works. To limit the 
potential impact to these speci es, TRCA will be planning for construction activities to occur outside of the 
bird breeding and snake hibernation windows.  
Wildlife/Bird Migration Patterns  
The breeding bird window occurs annually from April to late July. Any habitat destruction or heav y 
construction activity resulting in persistent disruption to habitat conditions during this timing window can 
affect breeding bird activities; birds may abandon their nests at will or choose not to nest in the vicinity 
at all. However, the proposed works affect a very localized area within the parkland corridor behind 
Peacham Crescent and will result in only minimal impacts to  bird habitat through the removal of select 
canopy trees. Additionally, construction activities will only occur outside of the bird breeding window . As 
such,  these proposed works should  not impact b reeding bird activities.  
Exotic/Alien and Invasive Species  
The 2018 ecology survey identified 16 exotic plant species within its expanded Study Area , accounting for 
37% of the plant biodiversit y within this geographic range. Two exotic vegetation communities were 
identified within the Project area but are limited to the lower reaches of the Project limits and do not exist 
on the slope behind Peacham Crescent . Levels of disturbance res ulting from t hese exotic species and 
communities throughout the ecology Study Area were observed to be moderate to severe. One of the 
main objectives of the proposed works is to enhance naturalization of the Project area. Thus, the proposed 
works will resu lt in the removal of exotic species within the Project limits and additional plantings of 
suitable native species.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    22 
 Wildlife Populations  
There are no significant  wildlife populations identified within the Project limits; therefore, there are no 
anticipated  impact s resulting from  the proposed works.  
Wetlands  
There  were two wetland vegetation communities observed along the southern limits of the Project area, 
only one of which is a native wetland community . Although the  wetland  vegetation communities are 
within the boundaries of the Project limits, they are physically divided from where slope stabilization 
works are planned to occur by an existing, highly vegetated land buffer  that acts as a natural protective 
barrier. This buffer,  in conju nction with effective use of  temporary erosion control measures during 
construction activities , will prevent any impacts to these wetland vegetation communities resulting from 
the proposed works.  
Microclimate  
There is no microclimate within or adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impacts 
resulting from the proposed works.  
Unique Habitats  
There are no unique habitats within or adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impacts 
resulting from the proposed works.  
Areas o f Natural Scientific Interest - Life Science (ANSI -LS) 
There are no life science areas of natural scientific interest  within or adjacent to the Project limits ; 
therefore, there will be no impacts resulting from the proposed works.  
3.1.3 Cultural  Environment 
Traditional Land Uses  
This Project is located within the Traditional Territories and Treaty Lands of the of the Mississaugas of the 
Credit First Nation, Williams Treaties First Nations, and the Huron -Wendat Nation. No adverse effects on 
traditional land uses were identified during engagement with Indigenous communities.  
Indigenous Reserve or Community  
There are no known Indigenous reserves or communities within the Project limits; therefore, there will be 
no impact s due to these  works.  
Outstand ing Native Land Claim or Treaty Rights  
The Project is located within the boundaries of the Toronto Purchase Treaty and is adjacent to a tributary 
of Black Creek, which is subject to the 2016 Water Claim filed by Mississaugas of the Credit First Nation . 
Transboundary Water Management Issues  
There are no transboundary water management issues within the Project limits; therefore, there will be 
no impacts resulting from the proposed works.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    23 
 Riparian Uses  
There are no significant riparian uses within the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impacts resulting 
from the proposed works.  
Recreational or Tourist Uses of a Waterbody and/or Adjacent Lands  
The adjacent parkland corridor behind Peacham Crescent  is actively used  for recreational purposes. There 
are three public parks within this corridor: Heathrow Park, Exbury Park, and Giovanni Caboto Park. There 
is a paved pedestrian pathway that runs through the parkland corridor and connect s all three parks.  
The location of the construction access route, st ockpile and staging area s for the proposed works will have 
significant, but temporary , impacts to the accessibility and use of Exbury park and will also result in the 
temporary closure of a segment of pathway that runs along the length of the southern Proj ect limit and 
construction access route . TRCA will post construction notices and trail closure signs to inform the public 
of temporary restrictions and estimated construction timelines.  
Aesthetic or Scenic Landscapes or Views  
There are no aesthetic or scenic landscapes or views within the Project limits; therefore, there will be no 
impacts resulting from the proposed works.  
Archaeological Resources, Built Heritage Resources and Cultural Heritage Landscapes  
The potential for archaeological r esources within the Project limits  were assessed through the 
examination of the historical and cultural context of populations that lived within and adjacent to the 
Project area. The archaeological study determined that there are no previously identified archaeological 
sites within one kilo meter of the study area and no known built or cultural heritage resources within the 
Project limits . However,  the characteristics of the  Project site indicate a potential for buried  cultural 
resources  to be present and warrant a Stage 2 Archaeological Asse ssment for certain areas within the 
Project limits . 
The Project site is situated proximal to Black Creek, within the Humber River Valley. Historically, this valley 
system offered rich natural resources such as fish, waterfowl and game which would have like ly been 
exploited by Indigenous peoples . The assessment also confirmed the existence of former historic 
homesteads, orchards, and laneways  belonging to  settlement communities . This historical context and 
evidence thus indicate  potential for the presence of  buried cultural artifacts within the limits of the Project 
area.  
A Stage 1 & 2 Archaeological A ssessment was undertaken in 2017 for a selective area within the Project 
limits and confirmed that no heritage resources will be impacted by the proposed works. However, an 
additional  Stage 1 assessment was conducted in 2018 to encompass  an expanded study area that 
capture d the full extent of the work, access route, and construction stagi ng areas. These  additional areas 
within the Project limits are  recommended  to undergo a Stage 2 assessment prior to any ground 
disturbing activities.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    24 
 Historic Canals  
There are no historic canals  within or adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, there wi ll be no impacts 
resulting from the proposed works.  
Federal Property  
There is no federal property within or adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impacts 
resulting from the proposed works.  
Heritage River System  
The Black Creek tributa ry that runs through the parkland corridor where the Project site is located , is not 
designated as part of a heritage river system ; therefore, there will be  no impacts resulting from the 
proposed works.  
3.1.4 Socioeconomic Environment  
Surrounding Neighbourhood or Community  
The neighbourhood surrounding the Project limits is comprised predominantly of single -detached  family 
homes  and is classified as low to medium residential landuse . According to the 2016 Census, each 
household  has an a verage of three (3) residents  and a categorically middle -class average household 
income  (Statistics Canada, 2016) . 
Surrounding Land Uses and Growth Pressure  
Situated in the City of Toronto, the Project area is subject to significant pressures resulting fro m the 
development of the surrounding urban environment. As can be observed  in historical aerial photographs  
shown in Figures 8 - 12, the region surrounding the Project limits has been subject to significant 
urbanization over the past 70+ years.  Urbanization changes the composition of the earth’s natural surface 
and typically results in the severe limitation or complete eradication of natural areas , resulting in 
increased stress es on remaining natural areas and features  impacting the health and w ellbeing of local 
ecosystems . 
The land  use currently surrounding the Project site is predominantly low  to medium density  residential 
space . Much of the surface area within the surrounding community  is comprised of impervious surface 
material (i.e. driveway s, roads, sidewalks, permanent built structures, etc.) . Impervious surfaces 
drastically affect the way in which water flow s and drain s over the surface , changing the dynamics of the 
hydrological system and  resulting in increased  impacts of erosion and pollution to  local ecosystems.
