 3.1.5 Engineering/Technical  Environment  
Rate of Erosion in Ecosystem  
The potential for erosion and slope instability within a local ecosystem is greatly influenced by the  type of 
landuse of the  surrounding areas . Although the Project site is surrounded by urban landuse, the overall 
extent of erosion and slope instability within the ravine system behind Peacham Crescent is  low due to 
the existence of dense vegetation  along the ravine slopes . The established tree and undergrowth 
communities within the parkland corridor greatly help to disperse the energy of overland runoff, secure 
soil materials , and contribute to water absorption,  ultimately reducing the overall impact of erosional 
forces. TRCA has been actively monitoring the rate of erosion behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent 
since the occurrence of the initial failure on July 8, 2013. The proposed works will ultimately result  in 
further enhancement to  erosion control and slope stability capabilities within the local ecosystem . 
Sediment Deposition Zones in Ecosystem  
There are no sediment deposition zones within the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impacts 
resulting from the proposed works.  
Flood Risk in Ecosystem  
There is a very low flood risk within the ecosystem  as mapped flood lines do not extend into the tributary 
channel and can only be approximated . The Black Creek tributary that runs behind Peacham C rescent is 
generally low-flow and has been integrated into the local stormwater management system and 
subsequently designed to accommodate surplus overland runoff from  adjacent  urbanized lands . Should 
flooding occur, t he steep ravine slopes would act as a barrier to help contain rising water levels  and protect 
structures at the top of the ravine; however, there could be potential for damage to the existing paved 
pathway and pedestrian bridges. The proposed works will result in improvements to  local flood risk as 
overland runoff and drainage will be better controlled within the Project limits.  
Slope Stability  
The bottom of slope is heavily treed and densely vegetated and was identified in the 2015 SS&ERA to be 
well stabilized. The upper slope, however, has already succumbed and continues to be at risk of slope 
instability due to factors including, but not limited to, an over steepened slope face and overloading of 
the upper sl ope with extensive fill material. The proposed works will address this upper slope instability 
by lightening the load at top of slope through the remov al of unregulated  fill material, regrading the upper 
slope to a more stable inclination, installing a mec hanically stabilized retaining structure, and re-establish 
vegetation along  the upper slope to help increase soil strength.  
Existing Structures  
There are three (3) detached private  residential dwellings at the top of slope within the Project limits. One 
(1) of these residences is at long -term risk from the hazards of erosion and slope instability should no 
remedial works take place. As such, the proposed slope stabilization solution will provide protection to 
this at -risk residence in addition to providing protection to the existing tableland  behind all three (3) 
residences from the hazards of erosion and slope instability.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    33 
 Hazardous Lands/Sites  
Property or land that could be unsafe for development because of naturally occurring processes 
associated with flo oding, erosion, dynamic beaches or unstable soil or bedrock (Conservation Authorities 
Act, 1990)  is defined as hazardous lands  and would include the valley slope behind the affected properties 
on Peacham Crescent.  
The hazardous lands where the proposed sta bilization works are located are to be transferred to TRCA as 
the benefiting landowner's contribution to the project; to permit TRCA to monitor and maintain the 
completed works; to foster resilient ravine systems; and to expand public greenspace.  
4.0 E VALU ATION OF PRELMINARY ALTERNATIVE OPTIONS  
The information obtained in the baseline inventory is used in the evaluation of the preliminary alternative 
options, giving specific consideration of the advantages and disadvantages of each method.  
4.1 Description of Preliminary Alternative Options  
The following are the alternative conceptual solutions that were developed and evaluated as part of the 
Class EA process to address the identified upper slope failure behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent, in  
the City of Toronto. The alternative solutions were developed by Terraprobe Inc. (2019) and are based on 
topographic data provided by TRCA and the subsurface data collected from the  2015 SS&ERA conducted 
by Cole Engineering Group Ltd. Each proposed concep tual alternative has been developed to stabilize the 
upper 3 –  6 metres of slope only. The lower slope has not been included as part of these remedial works 
since  it is well vegetated , there are no  significant signs of toe erosion or slope instability, and  ultimately  
minimize the degree of disturbance to the valley lands behind Peacham Crescent.  
Alternative 1  – ‘Do Nothing’  
Alternative 2  – Regrade and revegetate  
Alternative 3 –  Mechanically Stabilized Earth (MSE) structure  
The evaluation of each preliminary  alternative option includes an examination of the types and extents of 
impacts, both positive and negative.  
4.1.1 Alternative 1 - ‘Do Nothing’  
The “Do Nothing” option is a mandatory alternative that must be considered during the Class EA process 
in order to justify the need to undertake remedial works within the Project limits. Should the “Do Nothing” 
option or other CA programs such as land acquisition be deemed a more acceptable solution, then there 
shall be no further consideration for remedial action and the Class EA process terminates.  
Prior to making a decision to proceed with remedial action at the site, TRCA evaluated the ‘Do Nothing’ 
option, which assesses the impacts risks over time to the proposed site if no remedial works were 
undertaken. If no remedial action is taken, it is anticipated that there is a higher likelihood for a substantial 
loss of tableland, landscaping structures, and ongoing risk to life and property.  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    34 
 Considering the potential risk to life and property, which would result if a ‘Do Nothing’ approach was 
taken, TRCA made the determination that this was not a viable option . 
4.1.2 Alternative 2 – Regrade and Revegetate  
This alternative would require the upper 3 – 6 metres  of the slope face (comprised mainly of fill material) 
behind 21, 23, and 25  Peacham Crescent to be cut- back and regrad ed (shaped) into a more gradual, stable 
slope inclination. The lower slope will not be subject to regrading as it is naturally stabilized by existing, 
dense vegetation. A ll efforts will be made to not disturb the existing vegetation along the lower slope  that 
provide this stabilization . Following regrading, the upper slope surface would be well- vegetated with 
suitable native species . The planted vegetation will provide increased  soil stabilization , reduce surficial 
erosion,  and add to the biodiversity of the local ecosystem  once it becomes  established . Unfortunately, i t 
will be necessary for the regraded slope to blend  into the rear yards of 21, 23 , and 25 Peacham Crescent, 
effectively pushing the slope crest back towards the dwellings and reducing the amount of useable 
tableland in the rear of these properties. Regrading will also result in significant disturbances to existing 
vegetation  along the upper portion of the slope , including mature trees, and will require excess soil 
disposal.  
4.1.3 Alternative 3 – Mechanically Stabilized Earth (M SE) Structure  
The MSE structure will consist of either a vegetated slope or a vegetated wall  system . The MSE slope 
would reconstruct the upper 4 –  5 metre portion of slope behind 21, 23, and 25 Peacham Crescent into 
an appropriate slope inclination to supp ort the MSE slope structure. The MSE  wall would consist of 
reconstructing the same extent of upper slope except with a slightly steeper slope inclination to support 
the construction of a terraced (tiered) wall . The MSE slope and wall systems would both be constructed 
out of reinforced fill F lex MSE bags and plates with geogrids suitable to support the growth of vegetation.  
Similar to Alternative 2, reconstruction of the  lower extent of the slope i s not i ncorporated into this 
alternative and all efforts will be made to keep the existing vegetation along the lower slope undisturbed 
in order to retain the existing natural slope stabilization. The impacts to existing vegetation and amount 
of soil removed from the upper slope in this alternative is similar to that of Alternative 2 ; however,  this 
alternative has the advantage of eliminating the loss of tableland from the rear of 21, 23, and 25 Peacham 
Crescent.  
4.2 Evaluation  of Preliminary Alternative Options  
As part of the Class E nvironmental Assessment process, each of the identified alternatives is rated against 
a consistent set of evaluation criteria set out by CO’s Class EA for Remedial Flood and Erosion Control 
Projects. The evaluation criteria typically include consideration for technical, economic, environmental 
and social factors or objectiv es. The evaluation of alternative solutions consisted of a qualitative ranking 
that considered the impact or effect of each alternative would have on technical, economic, 
environmental, and social factors . An evaluation matrix (Table 1 ) and a pros and cons  matrix ( Table 2 ) 
were  developed summarizing the extent of potential impacts for each alternative .

4.3 Selection of the Preferred Alternative  Option  
Alternative 3 –  Mechanically Stabilized Earth (MSE) Structure  
The results of the evaluation of alternatives led to the identification of Alternative 3 : MSE Structure  as the 
preferred alternative for the  Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Project . Alternative 3 is anticipated to have a 
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    38 
 less impact on  existing vegetation and excess soil removal than  Alternative 2  and provide s an  opportunity to 
address drainage and stormwater runoff concerns  within the Project limits.  
This selected alternative meets all  three  (3) of the Project objectives:  
Objective 1  – Risk mitigation  
The preferred alternative incorporates  a vegeta ted slope stabilization solution  that will effectively reduce 
future surficial erosion and provide long- term , low maintenance  protection to the dwellings at top of slope and 
incid ental protection of the pedestrian pathway at bottom of slope. The proposed works thereby effectively 
protects properties and human life from the hazards of erosion and slope instability.  
Objective 2  – Naturalization 
The preferred alternative supports the native terrestrial ecosystem habitat by supporting local biodiversity and 
resiliency through the incorporation of native vegetation within the structure design . The face of the MSE 
structure is designed to support the growth of vegetation and will be seede d with native seed mix during 
installation. Additionally, r estoration works are planned to follow construction activities to effectively restore 
disturbed areas and further support the naturalization of the local ecosystem  through the plantings of suitable  
native species . 
Objective 3  – Compatibility  
While evaluating alternatives, preference was given to alternatives which would have a minimal construction 
footprint and machine access requirements in effort to reduce impacts to the surrounding natural and built 
environments. Resulting impacts to public  safety  was also considered during the evaluation process.  
5.0 ENVIRONMENTAL SCREENING  
5.1 Detailed Environmental Analysis  of the Preferred Alternative Options  
To complete the detailed environmental analysis of the preferred alternative, the information c ollected for the 
baseline inventory is examined in greater detail to confirm potential impacts, refine methods of mitigation, and 
to identify any unforeseen impacts. The evaluation of impacts includes both temporary impacts during 
construction of the under taking, and permanent impacts due to function and maintenance of the works after 
construction.  Table 3 evaluates  the potential negative and positive effects of the proposed undertaking on the 
environment during construction and maintenance phases. It inclu des consideration of the magnitude, 
geographic extent, duration, frequency, permanence or reversibility and ecological context of the effects, as well 
as proposed mitigation measures and any residual effects.  
Environmental components that have been identi fied to potentially affect  the environment, both positively  and 
negative ly, are discussed herein. Those that have been determined as not applicable (n/a) have been omitted 
from further discussion . 
Screening of potential effects are considered to be  negative ( -), neutral (NIL) or positive (+) and are rated as 
relatively high (H), medium (M), low (L) or not applicable (NA) ( CO, 2013).

 6.1 Public Notifications and Consultation  
In accordance with the Class EA process, the first point of public contact occurred with the publication of the 
Notice of Intent on November 22, 2018 .  
Notices were sent to community g roups, homeowners in the vicinity of the Project site, I ndigenous communities, 
and government regulatory agencies including CO , MECP, MNRF, and the City of Toronto . 
The NOI requested that individuals contact the Project Manager if they wish to participate further in the EA 
process or to be kept informed about the Project’s status.  
A summary of the individuals and community groups that expressed interest in the Projec t after distribution of 
the NOI is summarized in Appendix D . 
6.1.1 Role of the Community Liaison Committee (CLC)  
The Project aligns with the following excerpt from CO’s Class EA document:  
“In an effort to facilitate more on- going public involvement at the project level, the Conservation 
Authority shall, based on its contact group mailing lists and expressions of interest from the local 
landowners, members of the general public, interest groups, or agencies, establish a Community 
Liaison Committee (CLC) to a ssist the Authority by obtaining additional public input concerning the 
planning and design process of an individual flood and/or erosion control project, and to review 
information and provide input to the Conservation Authority throughout the process. The  Conservation 
Authority shall strive to ensure that the membership of the CLC is representative of all views respecting 
a proposed remedial and erosion control project.  
As the name implies, the function of the CLC, in the Class EA process, will be to assis t the Conservation 
Authority to reach out and maintain contact with community residents, groups, associations and 
organizations. The CLC will provide direct input into the process. At the end of the process, the entire 
committee will have been exposed to t he entire process, will have understood how decisions have been 
reached and will have had their questions answered during the process.  
To fulfill its function, the CLC will:  
• Identify items of public concern about  the impact and design of proposed erosion 
control alternatives;  
 
• Provide direct input on these concerns to the Conservation Authority to be utilized 
throughout the planning and design process;  
 
• Co-host, with Authority Staff, meetings organized by the Authority to facilitate the 
resolution of concerns relating to a proposed remedial work;  
 
• Review any Part II Order Requests made by members of the public and attempt to 
resolve the issues of concern between the Part II Order requesters and the Conservation  
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project  
  
     Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    43 
 Authority before the request gets referred to the Minister of the Environment for a 
decision; and 
 
• Where appropriate, submit an assessment to the Conservation Authority, upon project 
completion, commenting on the effectiveness of the Class EA process for meeting public 
concerns for the specific project, and where relevant, identify possible improvements 
(CO, 2013).”  
6.1.2 CLC Meeting #1  
The first CLC meeting was hosted by TRCA on March 7, 2019 at TRCA’S Head Office, located at 101 Exchange 
Avenue, in th e City of Vaughan.  TRCA delivered a presentation outlining the events following the July 8, 2013 
severe storm event, TRCA’s response to the storm, the findings of the detailed slope stability and erosion risk 
assessment at 21, 23, and  25 Peacham Crescent, an evaluation of preliminary alternative solutions, and the 
proposed next steps. The CLC meeting served as a forum for interested residents and stakeholders to ask 
questions of senior project staff regarding the proposed erosion control and slope stabilization works, including 
timelines.  
Comment forms were distributed by TRCA following the CLC meeting to ensure an understanding of the Project 
objectives, to solicit input into the planning and design process, and to address any questions or concerns 
identified by stakeholders about the preliminary alternative options being evaluated. Concerns from affected 
landowners included  but were not limited to:  
• Proposed finished grades of rear y ards at affected properties on Peacham Crescent;  
• Cost comparison;  and 
• Responsibility of maintenance and repairs . 
 
Documentation related to this CLC meeting is contained in Appendix D . 
6.2 Indigenous Engagement  
Indigenous Communities have a range of views and experience to contribute to a project, thus engagement may 
take on different forms in each community, depending on both the scope of the project and interests of the 
community. Indigenous Engagement is intended to provide Indigenous Communities with an opportunity to 
receive information about and have input to the project  proposal and, equally allows TRCA to identify and 
consider the concerns and issues of those communities.

the lists held by Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA). The following communities and agencies

8.0 MONITORING PROGR AM 
Once construction of the works is deemed complete, regular inspections will be completed by staff through 
TRCA’s long -standing Erosion Management Pr ogram to ensure the works are performing as expected. These 
inspections will flag any maintenance that may be required so it can be scheduled and carried out in a timely 
manner. The erosion control and slope stabilization structure shall be included in TRC A’s Stream and 
Infrastructure Database (SEID) where construction specifications, photos, and structure conditions will be 
documented. Visual inspections will be completed following major storm events for a 1 -year period. Site 
inspections will be conducted annually until a 3-year period has passed, after which time  inspections will be 
adjusted to an appropriate frequency depending on structure’s condition. Copies of these inspection reports can 
be provided to parties of interest upon formal request to TR CA’s Engineering Projects group.  
If a significant deviation from expected performance is noted during a visual inspection, additional surveys will 
be undertaken immediately. If a survey detects a significant deviation from expected performance, then 
maintenance will be planned and implemented on a priority basis to the limit of available funding each year.   
Class EA Project Plan: Peacham Crescent Erosion Control and Slope Stabilization Project

GERMAN MILLS SETTLERS PARK SANITARY INFRASTRUCTURE PROTECTION PROJECT –  SITE 2/3    
 
Municipality:  York Region ( City of Markham)  CFN:  50675  
Account code:  Child Account 189-35  2021 
Budget:  YR Levy = $72,000 
DMAF =  $10,000 
Funding source:  Parent  Account  18915 – York Integrated Infrastructure Protection Works Program  
(2021 York Region Capital Budget)  
Current Status:  Active planning ; design  phase, permitting and approvals.  
     
BACKGROUND  
TRCA, in association with the Regional Municipality of York ’s Environmental Services , is preparing to implement 
sanitary infrastructure protection works  to prevent exposure of the Leslie Collector sewer  along a reach of 
German Mills Creek in Markham . TRCA’s Infrastructure Hazard Monito ring Program (IHMP) has identified and 
monitored several at -risk sanitary sites along German Mills Creek  since 2011. Following a geomorphic study 
conducted in 2015 to assess and prioritize risks to infrastructure , four key areas were identified along this reach 
of the cr eek, which runs through German Mills Settlers Park and Bercy Wycliff Park. As City of Markham 
stormwater and trail infrastructure in the area will benefit from the proposed works, the City will also be cost 
contributing to  implementation of  the project.  
 
Sites 2 and 3 are the focus of the current project and include three high priority hazard sites (I -152, P -080, and 
P-068) where active channel migration and bed/bank erosion have placed sanitary sewer infrastructure at risk 
of exposure. Also in the project area are three lower priority infrastructure hazard sites (P -062, P -065, I -151), an 
at-risk pedestrian bridge abutment, and several areas where there is a risk to the pedestrian trail. A Schedule B 
Municipal Class Environmental Assessment (MCEA) initiated in 2018 determined that the preferred alternative to comprehensively address these risks was "Channel Realignment with Pedestrian Bridge Extension". S torm 
events in summer 2018 accelerated the erosion at I -152 and exposed the maintenanc e hole chimney , so interim 
emergency works were implemented to stabilize the hazard site .  
 
TRCA retained Greck & Associates in 2019 to develop detailed designs for Site 2/3 based on the preferred 
alternative . The draft design, which  involves  major channel realignment, the installation of localized erosion 
control structures with woody habitat features, wetland offsetting, and replacement of the pedestrian bridge,  is 
now at the 90% stage .  
 
Scheduling implementation so as not to have prolonged trail closures during the busier times of the year is one 
of the main complicating factors in the planning of the project. Con struction timing will also have to be scheduled 
for the warmwater fisheries timing window from July 1
st to March 31st. As a result, cons truction is tentatively 
scheduled to commence September 2022.  
 
SCOPE OF WORK  
With the detailed design on track to be completed in 2021, TRCA will be focused on obtaining all necessary 
permits and approvals required to  implement the proposed works.  This includes internal review for a TRCA 
permit , extending archaeology clearance, and  a Fisheries Act Authorization (FAA) permit . Internal Circulation #3 
for the 90% detailed design is to be completed in Winter/Spring 2021 and comments provided to  Greck & 
Associates , so revisions may be included in the final detailed design.  Work in support of the FAA application will 
also be conducted, including substrate mapping of German Mills Creek  by AMM staff.  There are no required 
MCEA deliverables for 2021.  
 
Tender  preparation is also scheduled to begin late 2021,  once the final design has been received . This will likely 
be limited to the development of Request for Pre- Qualification (RFPQ) documents for 2021, pending budget 
    
2 of 4 availability and progress on obtaining approvals. Issuance of a Request for Tender (RFT) is tentatively scheduled 
for 2022, with plans for implementation to begin in September of 2022.   
 
PROJECT LOCATION  
 
 
RISKS  
Schedule Risks  
• Delays in obtaining permits and approvals  (especially Fisheries Act Authorization (FAA) from DFO ), including 
delays in obtaining a Letter of Credit for the FAA application 
• Discovery of culturally significant artifacts on site during archaeological assessment requiring additional assessment, appro vals, and/or change in scope  
• Delays in finalizing detailed designs if reviewer comments are difficult to implement or FAA application review requires significant changes to offsetting/restoration plans  
• Delays in beginning tender development or scheduling implementation due to changes in budget allocation 
for various ERM projects  or workload of Major Contracts staff  
 
Cost Risks  
• Changes in offsetting plan or cost of letter of credit following FAA application 
• Unforeseen design challenges or c hanges in scope, especially in restoration plans, based on requests  from 
municipal stakeholders  following review of final detailed design

on site (risk for 2022)

German Mills Settlers Park Sanitary Infrastructure Protection – Project Brief

1 INTRODUCTION  
Toronto and Region Conservation Aut hority  Erosion Risk Management (TRCA ERM ) and York Regio n 
Environmental Services (th e “Region ”) are preparing sanitary infrastructure  protection works in German  Mills  
Settlers Park  to prevent expos ure of the Leslie Collector sewer  within German Mills Creek. Th e proposed  works 
include a major channel realignme nt to protect the Region ’s sanitary  sewer, as well as provide secondary  benefits 
to protect City of Markham  stormwater  and trail infrastructure within the project area.  Figure 1 show s the general 
project area  includin g the locati on of the sanitary sewer line and creek al ignments over time.  
The channel realignment will address three high priority hazard sites : I-152, P -060, and P -068 (Figure 2 - Figure  4). 
It will also  help to mitigate future erosion conc erns at three low priority hazard sites ( Sites P -062, P -065, and I -
151) which are  within the project area . Replac ement of a  pedestr ian bridge just upstream  of site P -068 is also 
included in the scope of work, to prevent the existing south abutment from being outflanked and to increase 
hydraulic cap acity  (Figure 5).  
This Project Brief provides planning, approvals,  and implementation details to  supplement the  90%  Detailed 
Design drawings and D esign Brief report  prepared by  Greck and Associates Limited , as well as  the 2019  Sche dule 
B Municipal Class Environmental Assessment (MCEA) Project File prepared by Erosion Risk Management  staff .  
For more information on the Site Description and Baseline Environmental Conditions , see the Project File and 9 0% 
draft Design Brief.  PROJECT NAME  Germ an Mills Settlers Park Erosion Control Project  
REGION  York  Region  (Markham)  
TRCA DIVISION  Restoration and Infrastructure  
CFN 50675  
ACCOUNT  189-35

 6 SCOPE OF WORK  
The purpose of the proposed works is to provide a long- term solution for protect ion of  regional sanitary 
infrastructure and City  stormwater and  trail infrastructure. There is also a secondary op portunity to provide 
aquatic and terrestrial habitat improvements through site restoration  following implementation of the proposed 
work . In general, implementatio n of this project includes:  
• Channel  realignment for sewer and trail protection  
• Restor ation of  the new channel with:  
o 7 vegetated boulder revetments  
o 2 brush layering treatments  
o 8 bioengineered live stake banks with coir mat  
o 4 rootwad s – large woody debris habitat treatments  
o 3 log snag s – large woody debris habitat treatments  
o 4 poo l-riffle sequences  
o 1 reconstructed armourstone wall for maintenance hole protection  
o 2 oxbow/online  wetland  features  with leaky berms  
• Realign ment  and replacement of  the existing  trail crossing with a new 30 m free -span bridge  
• Tableland restorat ion, including:  
o Realign ment of  a portion of the multi- use recreational trail for the new bridge  location  
o Paving both new and damaged sections of trail  
o Stabilizing the site with turf seed along the trail and native seed for natural rest oration in all other 
areas  distur bed by the work  
o Planting of native trees and shrubs  
Please refer to Table 4.1: Proposed Bank Treatment Measures in the D esign  Brief (Greck and Associates Limited, 
2020)  for more details.   
 Required Tree Removals  
To facilitate site access and construction, 105 trees with an average DBH of 27  cm will need to be removed . Tree 
species to be removed include Manitoba maples, a variety of willow species, silver maples, black walnuts, and 
several other species.  Following TRCA’s replication ratio standards, 92 trees  and 2291 shrubs  are anticipated to 
be planted during site restoration  to compensate for the removals . Please refer to  Section 2.4 of the D esign  Brief 
(Greck  and Associates Limited, 2020)  for further details.   
 Vibrat ion and Construction Impacts  
Due to the location of the project, there is no  anticipated  risk to adjacent properties or structures  from vibration 
or other  construction impacts. The  Contractor will be required to complete their own risk mitigation  assessment 
and determine whether a risk mitigation plan  outlining infrastructure protection measures is required. The 
Contractor will also be required to  provide results  of pre - and post -construction CCTV inspections  of buried 
sanitary and stormwater  sewer  infrastructure.  
German Mills Settlers Park Sanitary Infrastructure Protection – Project Brief  
8 
 7 EROSION AND SEDIMENT CONTROL MEASURES  
Prior to the start of construction, the staging and stockpiling area will be isolated with standard sediment control 
fencing to prevent entry of deleterious substances into the watercourse as outlined in Dwg. No. ESC-2 (Sheet 3) 
of the detaile d design package . All barriers will be inspected and maintained in proper working order throug hout 
the duration of construction and  will remain in place until the site is sufficiently stabilized.  
All waste materials will be removed from the work site to prevent them from entering the watercourse.  
At the end of each working day, exposed soils shall be stabilized with appropriate erosion control matting where 
appropriate. Construction should be phased in a manner that limits the size of disturbed areas and minimizes 
clearing /grading. In general, excavation should be limited to an area that can be stab ilized by the end of the day 
to reduce the risk of leaving soil exposed for a  prolonged period . All disturbed areas shall be stabilized as soon as 
possible following co nstruction and restored  according to the restoration plan . 
8 MEASURES TO PROTECT FISH AND FISH HABITAT  
The following measures will be used to protect any fish and their habitat  in the project area:  
• A fish removal/rescue exercise will be carried out prior to commencing work within isolated areas , to 
ensure that any trapped fish are safely released immediately downstream of the isolated area. Exercise is 
to be completed by TRCA staff under  a permit from MNR F. 
• Existing  trails, roads, and cut lines will be used wherever possible to avoid excess disturbance of riparian  
vegetation.  
• The remov al of existing vegetation will be kept to an absolute minimum.  
• Staff will minimize the required works within the watercourse and along  its banks. Construction shall 
proceed in a continuous fashion  to minimize the duration of work.  
• All in  stream work is to be completed during periods of low flow only.  
• Any water intake or outlet pipes/hoses shall have screens to prevent the entra inment or impingement of 
fish and follow the measures as outlined in DFO’s Freshwater Intake End of Pipe Fish Screen Guideline . 
• As stone  will be used during construction of the riffles, walls, weirs, the vegetated rock buttress, and the 
plunge pool, the following measures will be taken  to protect fish habitat:  
o Appropriately  sized, clean rock will be placed to st abilize the area of concern.  
o Rocks  will not be obtained from below the ordinary high -water mark of any water body.  
o Rock that is acid generated will be avoided, as will rock that factures and breaks down quickly 
when exposed to the elements.  
o Rock will be in stalled at a similar slope to maintain a uniform stream width.  
German Mills Settlers Park Sanitary Infrastructure Protection – Project Brief  
9 
 o Rock will not interfere with fish passage or constrict the channe l width.  
• Machinery  will be kept in clean condition and in good working order to prevent the entry of any 
deleterious substances into the watercourse . Machinery shall be refueled no less than 30 metres from the 
watercourse . 
9 CONSTRUCTION BEST MANAGEMENT PRAC TICES  
The proposed construction phasing sequence, as discussed in  Section 6, conside rs site constraints, access routes, 
staging and storage requirements, and construction timing windows . The plan ensures that existing fl ows are 
maintained downstream of the work area, without interruption , during all stages of construction. The following 
best management practices (BMPs) will be employed during construction to ensure  ongoing protection of the 
environment and public safe ty: 
• A spill kit shall be kept on site at all times , in the event of a s pill resulting from machine breakdown or 
damage.  
• Tree protection barriers will be erected where necessary and will be void of any grade changes or storage 
of materials/equipment wi thin the Tree Protection Zone (TPZ).  
• All disturbed areas are to be resto red to existing conditions or better. Disturbed areas will be vegetated 
by planting and seeding with native trees, shrubs , and grasses to prevent erosion. In the event that there 
is insufficient time remaining in the growing season, the site will be temporarily stabilized (e.g. exposed 
areas  will be covered  with erosion control blankets to keep soil in place ) and the plantings will be 
completed during the next appropriate planting window. In accordance with this, effectiv e erosion and 
sediment control measures will be maintained until site is re -vegetated.  
• Machinery and vehicles will be kept clean of debris an d seeds  to prevent the spread of invasive species.  
10 CONSTRUCTION TIMING  
Implementation is tentatively sched uled  to commence September 202 2, pending receipt of approvals and budge t, 
and is anticipated to take approximately six-months duration . All in -stream works will be completed during low 
flow periods and within the designated fisheries constructio n timing  window , from July 1st to March 31st, to protect 
fish habitat. Please refer to Section 6.1 Fish Habitat During Construction of the Design Brief (Greck and Associates 
Limited, 2020) for further details.  
11 SITE RESTORATION AND MONITORING  
 Restoration Plantings  
As noted above, a total of 10 5 trees will need to be removed to facilitate construction.  This equal s 899  tree units  
to be replaced under T RCA’s 2018 Guid eline for Determ ining Ecosystem Compensation . For this project, six shrubs  
are considered  equal  to one tree unit under this compensation system. As a result,  it is tentatively  propos ed that 
German Mills Settlers Park Sanitary Infrastructure Protection – Project Brief  
10 
 92 trees  and 2291 shrubs will be  planted at the site (or in approved off-site locations)  to compensate for the 
removals.  The greater  shrub  compensation ratio has been  proposed  to infill compensation plantings  between  the 
existing mature vegetation within the project area . Asset owners have also directed that tree plantings are to be 
offset  from buried sanitary and stormwater sewers  infrast ructure , thereby further reducing the ability to plant 
significant  quanti ties of trees within the project area.  Nevertheless, th e proposed tree - to- shrub compensation  
ratio is under considera tion by the consulting team  and it is anticipated that th e ratio will be adjusted for the  final  
100% design circulation.  
All disturbed areas will be seeded with riparian , greenfield , or lawn  seed  mix during favourable weather conditions.  
For further details  regarding  site restoration and plantings,  please refer to Dwg. No. RP-1 and RP -2 (Sheets 11 & 
12) from the  detailed design package . 
 Post -Construction Monitoring  
The site will be inspected monthly  for the first year after construction  and following all major storm events  to 
ensure that the implemented works are stable and performing as designed . During the warranty period of the 
work, TRCA will inform the Contractor of any deficie ncies to be corrected.  Long- term site monitoring  will continue  
after completion of the warranty and regulator y compliance reporting  period  under the York Infrastruc ture  Hazar d 
Monitoring Pro gram (YIHMP) . YIHM P inspection s are conducted after all major storm events , as well as  on 1-, 2-, 
3- or 5-year cycle s determined by  structure condition rankings . Any maintenance required will be reported to the 
appropriate TRCA staff to ensure that deficiencies are corrected in a timely manner and to the extent of available 
funding.   
 Asphalt Paving  
The access route and project site are  to be restored to original condition s or better. Please refer to Dwg. No. SP -2 
(Sheet 5) from the detailed design package for more information  on proposed paving.

Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Projec t – Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    1  
1 INTRODUCTION  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) has proposed to undertake remedial slope stabilization 
works to address upper slope instability along a valley slope behind four  (4) private residential properties 
located at 21, 23, 25 , and 27 Peacham Cresc ent, in the City of Toronto. The project site is located northeast of 
Jane Street and Wilson Avenue within the Humber River Watershed.  
This project is being completed in accordance with the Class Environmental Assessment for Remedial Flood and 
Erosion Control Projects (Class EA) (amended 2013). The purpose of the Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization 
Project (hereafter referred to as “the Project” ), is to take action to protect human life and property from the 
hazards of valley slope erosion by providing long -term, low maintenance protection for the four  (4) private 
properties within the Project limits.  
Following the July 8, 2013, severe weather event, significant slumping and slope instability was reported behind 
Peacham Crescent (Figure 1 and Figure 2). After visual inspections by TRCA Erosion Risk Management staff, it 
was noted that a failure had occurred along the upper slope causing damage to an existing wooden retaining 
wall, causing lo ss of the tableland behind three properties. TRCA subsequently retained Cole Engineering Group 
Ltd., an engineering consultant, to conduct a slope stability and erosion risk assessment (SS&ERA). The purpose 
of the SS&ERA was to determine the extent o f risk to essential structures at this site. The SS&ERA was 
completed in 2015 and identified that one (1) private residential dwelling is at -risk should no remedial control 
works be undertaken.  PROJECT NAME  Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Project  
REGION  City of Toronto (North York)  
TRCA DIVISION  Restoration and Infrastructure  
CFN 49680.25  
ACCOUNT  133-35 
Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Projec t – Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    2

Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    3 2 SITE DESCRIPTION  
 Location  
The Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Project is located along Peacham Crescent, in the City of Toronto 
(North York)  as shown in Figure 3. The site involves four ( 4) prop erties (21, 23, 25 and 27 Peacham Crescent) 
that back onto City of Toronto Ravine property known as Exbury Park. The closest major intersection is Jane 
Street and Wilson Avenue . 
 
Figure 3. Approximate location of works behind Peacham Crescent.  Source: TRCA, 20 21. 

Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Projec t – Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    4 
 Land Usage  
As shown in Figure 4, medium residential is the most notable type of development in the surrounding 
community. The parkland  to adjacent to the project area consists of wooded parkland and manicured 
greenspaces wit hin Exbury Park.  
 
Figure 4 Categorized land use surrounding the Peacham Crescent Project  (denoted by red star ). Source: TRCA, 
2021. 
 ANSI/ESA Areas  
There are no Environmentally Significant Areas (ESA) or Areas of Natural Scientific Interest (ANSI) within or 
adjacent to the Project limits; therefore, there will be no impact from the proposed works.  
 Existing Erosion Control Structures  
There are no existing erosion control structures within the project limits. There is a creek armoured with gabion 
basket walls that runs adjacent to  the project area , as shown in Figure 5. Additional gabion baskets and stone 
retaining walls are present on either side of the creek and along the construction access bridge as show in Figure 
6. This creek is located outside of the project limits ; therefore, there will be no  anticipated impact s resulting f rom the 
proposed works.

Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Projec t – Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    6 
 Typical Slope/Channel Dimensions  
The valley slope runs east to west along the southern limits of the affected private properties. The slope failure 
is generally localized to the upper  3 - 4 m of the slope where uncontrolled/un -engineered earth fill,  and 
damaged retaining walls are present. The middle and lower portion of the slope is generally well vegetated with 
numerous young and mature trees and under growth (Figure 7).  
For more detailed information on the typical slope dime nsions of the project area, please refer to Cole 
Engineering’s Slope Stability and Erosion Risk Assessment –  Area E Properties ; Peacham Crescent , City of  
Toronto 2015 report.  
 
Figure 7. Valley slope conditions, facing north.  Source: TRCA, 2020.  
 Flow Data  
By-pass pumping/de -water will not be  required as all work will take place on the slope/table land away from any 
significant watercourse.  
3 BASELINE INVENTORY  
The baseline environmental inventory takes into consideration the directly and indirectly affected environment. 
The area indirectly affec ted by the Project includes Exbury Park behind Peacham Crescent that connects 
Heathrow Park to Giovanni Caboto Park.

 Archaeological Assessment  
TRCA Archaeology staff completed a Stage 1 & 2 archaeological field investigation  on May 19, 2017 , and the 
report confirms that the proposed project area is free of archaeological concerns.  
 
For a full account of the archeological information and recommendations  for the project site, please refer to 
TRCA’s Archaeological Assessment (Stage 1 -2) for Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization  (2017) and HR20 -09 
Property Ass essment Results –  Additional Assessment (2020).  
 Built Features  
3.2.1 Parks and Trails  
Exbury Park features the Giovanni Caboto Park Trail which extends from Exbury Road to Jane Street. To the west 
of Jane Street,  the formal trail becomes known as the Black  Creek Trail.  
 Aquatic Inventory and Habitat  
A Black Creek tributary runs through the parkland corridor behind Peacham Crescent; however, the 2018 
ecology survey identified the tributary channel to be a turbid open aquatic environment (disturbed and 
unvegetated). These aquatic conditions are not suitable for fish habitation; therefore, there will be no impact to 
fish habitats resulting from the proposed works.  
The project does not include any in -water work  and does not correspond with an y Environmental Monitor ing 
and Data Management (EMDM ) Regional Watershed Monitoring Program stations .

additional properties. The appro ximate limits of the completed tree inventory are shown in Figure  8 below.

TRCA’s Environmental Monitoring and Data Management (EMDM) in 2018 to inventory existing flora, fauna, and 
vegetation communities within the study area. This data was gathered to inform planning and design activities 
and to ensure decisions are made with consideration of the local ecosystem.

5.2.3 Mitigative Measures  
The following notes must be adhered to when conducting works near infrastructure:  
• Constructure fencing should be implemented around infrastructure and light standards to ensure a safe 
buffer zone for site access.  
• The crossing of heavy equipment should be limited  near infrastructure . Equipment should be operated 
at a “dead slow” speed to minimize impact to the infrastructure.  
• Protection must be in place for any infrastructure and associated structures  where construction vehicles 
may cross in the work zone including, but not limited to, steel plates and/or ramping.  
• Meas urements should be taken to ensure there is hand digging only within 1 m circumference 
(horizontal and vertical) of infrastructure. All work within the 1 m horizontal zone should include 
spotters to ensure no damage is being caused to utilities.  
• No stockpi ling of materials or debris on infrastructure unless approved by the City. 
• No excavation over or exposure of the infrastructure (unless approved by the City  and in the presence of 
an inspector).  
• Monitoring of the condition(s) of the cover over the infrastr ucture shall occur regularly during the 
construction period (all locations that are being crossed and in particular the location where there is 
staging/stockpiling).  
Coordination with Municipal or Regional partners may be required. Emergency utilities cont act information is 
included in the Emergency Response Plan.

Peacham Crescent Slope Stabilization Projec t – Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    12 9 EROSION AND SEDIMENT CONTROL MEASURES  
Prior to the start of construction,  the Work Area  will be isolated with standard sediment control fencing to 
prevent the entry of deleterious substances into the watercourse. All barriers will be inspected and maintained 
in proper working order throughout the duration of construction and  will remain in p lace until the site is 
sufficiently stabilized.  
All waste materials will be removed from the work site to prevent them from entering the watercourse.  
At the end of each working day, exposed soils shall be stabilized with appropriate erosion control matting  where 
appropriate. Construction should be phased in a manner that limits the size of disturbed areas and minimizes 
clearing/grading. In general, excavation should be limited to an area that can be stabilized by the end of the day 
to reduce the risk of lea ving soil exposed for any prolonged duration. All disturbed areas shall be stabilized as 
soon as possible following construction and restored as described in Section 13 . 
 
The Erosion and Sediment Control Plan can be found within the detailed design drawing set prepared by 
Terraprobe Inc.  
10 MEASURES TO PROTECT FISH AND FISH HABITAT  
No in water works are proposed as part of this project. However, Erosion Sediment Control (ESC) will be 
installed on site to prevent deleterious materials form entering the nearby watercourse.  
11 CONSTRUCTION BEST MANAGEMENT PRACTICES  
The proposed construction phasing sequence, as discussed in  Section 6, recognizes site constraints, access 
routes, staging and storage requirements, and construction timing windows to facilitate the approved activities. 
The plan ensures that existing flows are m aintained downstream of the work area without interruption during 
all stages of construction. The following best management practices (BMPs) will be employed during 
construction to ensure the ongoing protection of the environment and public safety:  
 
• A spill kit shall be always kept on site in the event of a spill resulting from machine breakdown or damage.  
• Tree protection barriers will be erected where necessary and will be void of any grade changes and storage 
of materials/eq uipment within the Tree Protection Zone (TPZ).  
• All disturbed areas are to be restored to existing conditions or better. Any disturbed areas will be vegetated 
by planting and seeding with native trees, shrubs  and grasses to prevent erosion. If  there is insufficient time 
remaining in the growing season, the site will be stabilized e.g., cover exposed areas with erosion control 
blankets to keep the soil in place and prevent erosion and the plantings will be completed during the next 
appr opriate planting window. In accordance with this, effective erosion and sediment control measures will 
be maintained until site is re -vegetated.  
• Machinery and vehicles will be kept clean of debris and seeds to prevent the spread of invasive spec ies.

access route, as required .

1 Introduction  
The Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) retained Shoreplan Engineering Limited 
(Shoreplan)  for engineering services in support of the Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance 
Project . The TRCA request ed detailed designs for the protection and maintenance of this stretch 
of shoreline. This report documents  Shoreplan’s detailed design process for the site . Shoreplan 
visited the site several times  in preparation of the design for this report.  
The project site includes the shoreline of Humber Bay East Park from approximately the north east tip to just past the south west tip of the headland. The extent of the project site is outlined on 
Figure 1.1. The original scope of work has been split into 3 phases. This report details Phase 1, 
detailed design of shoreline protection for the eastern part of the site from  about the beach area 
on the south side to the headland and proceeding counter clockwise  to the north  easterly 
headland of Humber Bay Park East . Phase 2 will inclu de the detailed design for the southern 
headland.  Phase 3 will include the concrete rubble and gravel beach.  
1.1 Report Structure  
The report is organized as follows. Section 1 provides a brief introduction of the project. Section 
2 provides a review of the entire site. Section 3 is Shoreplan’s coastal analysis of the site. Section 
4 discusses the  detailed design of the shore protection for the entire site and the finalization of 
the design drawing for phase 1.  Associated estimated tonnages and/or volumes of materials  are 
also provided in Section 4.

2 Site Review  
2.1 Procedure  
The site is located within Humber Bay Park East along the lake shore of the City of Toronto, near 
the intersection of Marine Parade Drive and Lake Shore Boulevard West. A site review was 
completed by our staff on September  3 2019  and the site was visited by Shoreplan staff several 
times since then.  
2.2 Existing Physical Conditions  
The existing conditions were assessed through visual above and under water inspections during 
our visits, review of survey information and aerial photos. Topographic data and underwater 
contours were provided by TRCA.  
The shoreline at Humber Bay Park East consists of 2 headlands separated by a southeast facing 
beach. The length of shoreline to be protected, starting from the southern headland, is 
approximately 1200m. Each headland is currently protected by armour stone revetment structures that have been subjected to various degrees of damage during the recent high water levels. The 
beach profile has been eroded back.  The beach slope contains concrete rubble scattered over 
the gravel beach and nearshore area. A dive inspection completed in June 2020 noted that the 
shallow waters offshore contain rubble material of a variety of sizes up to approximately a 1m 
diameter.  This material does not extend very far offshore and that the lakebed quickly changes 
to consist of fine sand only. The slope offshore was noted as very gradual. A  collection of armour 
stones is scattered at the southern end of beach where a park’s pond outlets into the lake. These 
armour stones form the remnants of a protection structure for the creek outlet that has failed.  
This report discusses the condition and state of protection structures for the entire project area. 
Phase 1 of the detailed design drawings focuses on the protection structures for the northern headland.  On our design drawings, this area is represented by chainage as between stations 
0+550m and approximately 1+220m. Station 1+220m is located at the northern tip of the northern 
headland. The small beach to the southwest of this point is not part of the scope of this project . A 
multi- use trail runs along the entire shoreline of the northern headland. Some sections of the trail 
have been undermined due to the ongoing erosion.  
The shoreline is currently protected by  armour stone revetment structures.  The size of the armour 
stones , the crest elevations  and the nature of placement of the armour stones varies considerably.  
The armour stone sizes are most commonly in the 2 to 4 tonne range but larger stones up to 6 
tonnes, and some even larger ones, were found on the site. High water levels experienced 
between 2017 and 2019 have led to wave overtopping of the revetments  causing erosion behind 
the revetment structures , as well as erosion behind the beach area and loss of beach material . 
As a result, armour stones have shifted , slid down  or fallen back (landw ard), and lowered  the 
elevation of the structure, further reducing the efficacy of the protection structures.  Our 
observations indicate that most of the damage is in areas  with lower crest elevations and 
Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project

specifically in areas of lower crest elevation that are exposed to higher waves.  The wave 
distribution around the site is discussed in the next section.  
During our inspections it was noted that the majority of individual armour stones are in good 
condition and can remain in the revetment structures.  The below photos show the condition of the

3 Coastal  Assessment   
3.1 Water Levels  
Marine structures on the Canadian shores of the Great Lakes are typically designed using the 
100-year return period instantaneous water level for the design water level. The 100- year 
instantaneous water level commonly used in the Toronto area over the last few decades was 
75.7m above the Canadian Geodetic Vertical Datum of 1928 (CGVD28). That value was 
determined by MNR (1989), but  record high Lake Ontario water levels in 2017 and again in 2019 
have led to the reconsideration of design water levels.  
3.1.1  Extreme Value Analysis 
We completed new extreme value analyses of annual maxima hourly water levels measured at the Canadian Hydrographic Service water level gauge in Toronto Harbour, for the 58 -year period 
from 1962- 2019.  That covers the post -regulation period for Lake Ontario, as discussed in Section 
3.1.3 , below . Four probability distributions were considered (Gumbel, Weibull, Log Pearson- 3, 
and GEV) with Gumbel distribution providing the best fit to the data.  Figure 3-1 shows the results 
of the Gumbel analysis.  
TRCA’s planning department has moved towards adopting a design water level of 76.2m 
IGLD1985 . Figure 3-1 shows that elevation corresponds to the 100 -year return period water level 
at approximately the upper 70% confidence interval  from our analysis.  A project team decision 
was made to use that same design water level for this project in order to be consistent within TRCA’s different departments. A water level of 76.2 m IGLD1985 corresponds to 76 .15 m 
CGVD28. 
Figure 3-1 Toronto Water Level Extreme Value Analysis Results  
 

Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project  
Detailed Design Report (Final Phase 1)  TRCA Project No. 10020801 
Toronto Region Conservation Authority   Shoreplan File 19- 3133  
 
  9 
3.1.2  Climate Change  
A considerable amount of research has been done on climate change and its expected effects on 
the Great Lakes  and while results vary considerably, there is general consensus on several key 
points. Overall, water levels are expected to fall while severe storm frequency and intensity are both expected to increase. Climate change impacts on Lake Ontario water levels are expected to 
be less than on the other Great Lakes because its water levels are regulated.  
McDermid et al. (2015) synthesized available science on the observed and predicted impacts of climate change in the Great Lakes basin. They reported a lack of clarity in the understanding of multiple factors influencing water level projections for the Great Lakes, and a low confidence in the current projections of future water levels resulting from climate change. Given the low confidence in predicted future water levels we have not included specific climate change 
considerations in the assessment of design water levels.  
The extent to which the 2017 and 2019 high water levels can be attributed to climate change has 
been subject to some debate. Changes to Lake Ontario’s regulation plan, implemented in January 
2017, may have contributed to the 2017 record high water level. High precipitation levels in Lake 
Ontario’s watershed was the predominant cause of the high lake levels, but the extent to which 
that may be directly attributed to climate change has not been precisely quantified. Most available models suggest that Lake Ontario water levels will go down, but there is too much uncertainty in 
the current research.  
3.1.3  IJC Plan 2014  
The International Joint Commission (IJC) began regulating Lake Ontario water level in 1960 but 
soon re- evaluated their plans due to low water levels at Montreal. Plan 1958- D was implemented 
in October 1963 and was in effect until Plan 2014 was implemented in January 2017. Baird (2019) 
describes quarter -monthly Lake Ontario water levels simulations under Plan 1985DD and Plan 
2014 from 1900 to 2008, completed by Environment Canada. They recommended a 7cm increase 
in the design mean water level due to the expected impacts of the implementation of Plan 2014. The simulation results predicted that implementing Plan 2014 would lead to different increases in mean water levels, depending upon the return period considered. The increase at the 100- year 
return period was 0.07 metres.  
There is some uncertainty about how this 0.07m difference should be considered as part of an updated 100 -year instantaneous water level within the LOSH. Plan 2014 was in use when the 
2017 and 2019 high water levels occurred so its effect on those levels, whatever it may have 
been, are included in the updated extreme value analysis  described in 3.1.1 . The International 
Joint Commission has undertaken a review of Plan 2014 and has acknowledged the possibility 
that it could be changed, if required.  
In the absence of clear evidence regarding the impact of Plan 2014 on the 2017 and 2019 high 
water levels, and considering that IJC may change the plan, we have not considered changes due to the plan in our assessment of design water levels. It is worth noting, however, that our 
Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project  
Detailed Design Report (Final Phase 1)  TRCA Project No. 10020801 
Toronto Region Conservation Authority   Shoreplan File 19- 3133  
 
  10 
design assessment is based on the 70% confidence interval of the extreme value analysis, which 
is approximately 13cm higher than the estimated 100- year return period water level.

bathymetry.

3.3 Wave Hindcasting  
The hindcast  was used to provide hourly estimates of significant wave height, peak wave period 
and mean wave direction for the 46- year period from January  1, 1973 to December  31, 2018. The 
wave climate has a bi -nodal distribution with wave height and wave energy peaks in the easterly 
and southwesterly directions, as shown in Figure 3-3. Although the highest easterly wave is 
similar in height to the highest southwesterly wave, there are a significant number of higher waves coming from the east than the southwest. This can be seen from the wave height exceedance

curves in Figure 3-4, which show the percentage of time any given significant wave height was 
exceeded during the 47- year hindcast.

3.3.1  Design Waves  
Peak -over-threshold extreme value analyses were completed for both easterly and southwesterly 
storm events. The upper 90% confidence interval for the 100- year return period estimates of 
significant wave height and peak wave period were 5. 6m and 9.8s for an easterly wave and 4.4 m 
8.5s for a southwesterly wave.  Figure 3-5 and Figure 3-6 show the wave height contour and vector 
plot illustrating the significant wave heights at the site for the worst case directions, east and 
southwest winds. The maximum wave height in front of the shoreline was 3.46 m and has a peak 
wave period of 10.5 seconds. These values were used for design of new coastal protection 
structures .

4 Shoreline Protection Detailed Design  
Shoreplan has evaluated the existing site  conditions as described in Section 2, and considered 
the coastal conditions along the shoreline described in Section 3 of this report.  
4.1 Conceptual Design Options  
On the basis of the assessment and conditions, two conceptual approaches to remediation were 
considered for the revetment portions of the shoreline. These approaches included, as option 1, 
complete rebuil d of the revetment using, as much as possible the existing armour stone and 
supplement with additional armour as required, and, as option 2, supplementing the existing 
structures with additional armour stone while leaving the existing structures in place. A decision 
to proceed with option 2 was made in consultation with TRCA staff.  The main advantages of 
option 2 is substantial reduc tion of overall disturbance of the shoreline and likely eliminated risk 
of expos ure of existing fill material and overall reduced construction cost and time. Option 2 
includes a short section of the shoreline at the east side of the central beach where a new 
revetment is to be constructed.  
The beach stabilization options included a review of different size of beach materials  that could 
be used to supplement the existing beach profiles.  Details of the pond outlet structure located in 
the west part of the beach have been developed and are included as part of the beach sector. The outlet design is  site specific and involves a completely new construction.  
4.2 Phase 1 Detailed Design  
Detailed designs have been provided for all reaches of the shoreline with improved details for 
backfill, backshore grading and repair to the multi -use trail along the shoreline.  
The design of the revetments specifically considered the crest elevation and the crest width giving 
considerations to the existing backshore elevations. The objective of the crest design was to ensure unobstructed view of the lake, reduce the extent of backfill in the backshore that would impact existing vegetation, particularly mature trees, and ensure that overtopping rates were low 
enough to control backshore damage. The overtopping rate was limited to 10 l/m/s.   
Details of our design can be seen on the design drawings 19 -3133 r2 PHASE 1 sheets 1 to 5. In 
this report, the design will be discussed in separate sections for the south facing shoreline of the 
headland (stations 0+ 625m to 0+ 800m), the southern tip (s tations 0+ 825m to 0+925m ), east 
facing shoreline (s tations 0+ 950m to 1+175m ), and shoreline along the northern tip of the 
headland (s tations 1+200m to end). The cross sections of these reaches can be seen on sheets 
2 to 4 of the design drawings respectively.  
 
Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project  
Detailed Design Report (Final Phase 1)  TRCA Project No. 10020801 
Toronto Region Conservation Authority   Shoreplan File 19- 3133  
 
  15 
4.2.1  Stations 0+ 625m to 0+ 800m 
For the southern facing shoreline of the northern headland, the existing armour stone revetment 
needs  repair and  enhancem ent, and in the western end of this reach, in need of new construction  
due to the severity of erosion and lack of existing revetment structure. Design wave heights at 
this reach range from 2.2m to 2.6m.  
From stations 0+625 m to 0+ 800m the existing revetmen t structure is to remain in place and be 
supplemented with additional 2.0 -4.0 tonne armour stone placed at a 1.5H:1.0V slope.  The 
structure will rise to a crest elevation of 78.5m, where it will be topped with 3.0- 5.0m cap stones. 
The existing armour stones at the crest of the structure will be removed so the new cap stones 
can be founded on new rip rap. The width of the cap in various areas of this reach depends on the predicted wave overtopping of the structure, discussed in Section 4.3.1 . The backshore 
behind the structure will be raised with fill to an elevation of 77.75m, complete with topsoil and 
sod.  
4.2.2  Stations 0+ 825m to 0+925m 
Revetment structures are currently in place along the southern tip of the northern headland but 
have been damaged during recent high water levels and require repair and enhancement. 
Additional random placement armour stone, ranging from 2.0-4.0 tonne to 3.0-5.0 tonne 
dependant on the design wave height modelled at each location, will be placed along this reach of shoreline to enhance the existing revetment structures. The largest waves are  expected at the 
southern tip of the headland (0+82 0 to 0+925), as seen in Figure 3-5. The structure slope is 
changed to 2H:1V after station for 0+800m for increased stability of the armour stones. The crest elevation of the revetment structu re will rise from the 78.5m elevation of the previous reach as 
the structure continues along the southern tip, reaching a maximum crest elevation of 79.5m between stations 0+850 and 0+900, where wave heights are modelled to be the highest. The crest elevation decreases gradually to 78.0 m as the structure progresses along the shoreline from 
the southern tip towards the east facing shoreline. The crest of the structure along this reach 
features 3.0 -5.0 tonne cap stones underlain by 300- 600mm rip rap, ranging from 2 to 4m wide 
depending on expecting wave overtopping.  
4.2.3  Stations 0+950m to 1+ 175m 
Revetment structures are currently in place along the east facing shoreline of the northern headland but have been damaged during recent high water levels and require repair and enhancement. Additional random placement armour stone, ranging from 2 .0-4.0 tonne to 3.0 -5.0 
tonne dependant on the design wave height modelled at each location, will be placed along this reach of shoreline to enhance the existing revetment structures. The largest waves are expected at the northern tip of the headland (1+100m to 1+755m), as seen in Figure 3-5. The structure 
slope remains 2H:1V for increased stability of the armour stones. The crest elevation of the revetment structure is lowest between stations 0+975 and 1+025 (77.5m elevation) and gradually 
increases moving north as expected wave heights increase, reaching a maximum crest elevation 
of 78.5m at station 1+125, where wave heights are modelled to be the highest. The crest elevation 
Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project  
Detailed Design Report (Final Phase 1)  TRCA Project No. 10020801 
Toronto Region Conservation Authority   Shoreplan File 19- 3133  
 
  16 
decreases gradually to 77.8m as the structure progresses north along the shoreline. The crest of 
the structure along this reach features 3.0- 5.0 tonne cap stones underlain by 300- 600mm rip rap, 
ranging from 3 to 5m wide depending on expecting wave overtopping.  
4.2.4  Stations 1+200m to end 
As the revetment structure wraps west around the northern tip of the headland, expected wave heights become less severe. Additional random placement 2.0 -4.0 tonne armour stones will be 
placed to repair and enhance existing protection. The crest of the structure along this reach 
features 3.0-5.0 tonne cap stones approximately 2m wide and placed to a crest elevation of 77.3. 
The cap stones will be underlain by 300 -600mm rip rap.  
4.3 Design Considerations and Construction Costs 
4.3.1  Wave Overtopping  
The overtopping rate was estimated using the methods for armoured slopes and walls presented in the Overtopping Manual (EurOtop, 2018). For the revetment structures, the overtopping rate is 
calculated at the back of the crest of the structure.  
The crest elevation and width of the structure was established so that the estimated wave 
overtopping during design conditions would be approximately 10 l/s/m, a small amount that will be mitigated by the rip rap splash pad behind the cap stones. According to the EurOtop manual 
(2018), this value will  create minimal damage to the backshore area.  
4.3.2  Effects on Coastal Processes 
The new revetment structures planned for Phase 1 of this project are placed on the same footprint 
as the existing revetment structures  and therefore have a minimal impact on local coastal 
processes.  The new revetment structure placed between stations 0+550m and 0+650m will 
stabilize and prevent further erosion of the shoreline in that area. 
4.3.3  Stone Sizing  
Armour stone sizes were selected for stability using the Hudson equation and using the design 
coastal conditions detailed in Section 3 for design input.  
4.3.4  Cut and Fill Volumes  
Cutting into the bank was  minimized during the design process to avoid exposing potential 
contaminated material.  Additional fill material will be required behind the crest of the new 
revetment structures to tie into existing grades. Phase 1 will include , in additi on to stone materials 
listed in section 4.3.9, approximately  3,300  cubic meters  of fill material and 900 cubic meters of 
topsoil .  
Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance Project  
Detailed Design Report (Final Phase 1)  TRCA Project No. 10020801 
Toronto Region Conservation Authority   Shoreplan File 19- 3133  
 
  17 
4.3.5  List of Assumptions & Limitations  
There are limitations in evaluating the existing revetment structures. The thickness and toe depth 
of the existing armour stone and rip rap layers cannot be understood without dismantling the structure with large equipment.  Minimum layer thicknesses and bottom elevations for the 
reinforced revetment designs have been specified and it will be the responsibility of the contractor 
to ensure that the final structure is built to specifications.  
It was assumed that the native material composition of the shoreline had potential to be 
contaminated and therefore the protection structures were designed to minimize cutting and 
disturbing of native material.  
4.3.6  Aquatic Habitat Alteration & Loss  
The effect of proposed structure on aquatic habitat was evaluated in plan view to calculate  the 
areas of fish habitat that would be altered or lost as a result of the construction. The area of 
aquatic habitat lost, which is the area of the structure’s  footprint at the DFO high water mark (75.3 
m IGLD , 1985 ), is approximately 2,380 m
2. The area of altered habitat, measured as the structure 
footprint below elevation 75.3 m, would be approximately 2, 850m2.  
4.3.7  Vibrations from Construction  
Equipment to be used in the construction includes excavators, skid steers, and front end loaders. There is no requirement for equipment that would generate an excessive amount of vibration that 
would propagate beyond the construction area and cause any adverse effects.  
4.3.8  Vegetation Removal and Protection  
The proposed structure was designed to have minimal impact on local vegetation. Trees located 
at the crest of the existing shoreline where the new revetment structures are to be constructed 
will need to be removed or replaced. All other trees will be protected as part of the project.  
4.3.9  Volumes Estimate  
A construction materials  estimate was prepared for the design described above. The quantities 
were estimated using cross sectional areas of each design between stations 0+550m and 
1+200m. The volumes were calculated by multiplying each cross- sectional  area by 25m, the 
spacing between cross sections. The estimates are only for new materials to be added and do not include existing armour stone on site. A summary of the estimated tonnages  is provided in 
Table 4.1.  
The order of magnitude construction cost estimate is for the works proposed in Phase 1 is 
estimated to be $5, 500,000.  This amount does not include any contingencies or taxes.

1 Introduction  
The Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) retained Shoreplan Engineering Limited 
(Shoreplan)  for engineering services in support of the Humber Bay Park East Major Maintenance 
Project . The TRCA request ed detailed designs for the protection and maintenance of this stretch 
of shoreline. This report documents  Shoreplan’s detailed design process for the site . Shoreplan 
visited the site several times  in preparation of the design for this report.  
The project site includes the shoreline of Humber Bay East Park from approximately the north east tip to just past the south west tip of the headland. The extent of the project site is outlined on Figure 1.1. The original scope of work has been split into 3 phases. This report details Phase 1, 
detailed design of shoreline protection for the eastern part of the site from  about the beach area 
on the south side to the headland and proceeding counter clockwise  to the north  easterly 
headland of Humber Bay Park East  and. Phase 2, detailed design for the southern headland  
proceeding counter clockwise to the beach.  Phase 3 will include the concrete rubble and gravel beach.  
1.1 Report Structure  
The report is organized as follows. Section 1 provides a brief introduction of the project. Section 
2 provides a review of the entire site. Section 3 is Shoreplan’s coastal analysis of the site. Section 
4 discusses the  detailed design of the shore protection for the entire site and the finalization of 
the design drawing for phase 1.  Associated estimated tonnages and/or volumes of materials  are 
also provided in Section 4.

2 Site Review  
2.1 Procedure  
The site is located within Humber Bay Park East along the lake shore of the City of Toronto, near 
the intersection of Marine Parade Drive and Lake Shore Boulevard West. A site review was 
completed by our staff on September  3 2019  and the site was visited by Shoreplan staff several 
times since then.  
2.2 Existing Physical Conditions  
The existing conditions were assessed through visual above and under water inspections during 
our visits, review of survey information and aerial photos. Topographic data and underwater 
contours were provided by TRCA.  
The shoreline at Humber Bay Park East consists of 2 headlands separated by a southeast facing 
beach. The length of shoreline to be protected, starting from the southern headland, is 
approximately 1200m. Each headland is currently protected by armour stone revetment structures that have been subjected to various degrees of damage during the recent high water levels. The 
beach profile has been eroded back.  The beach slope contains concrete rubble scattered over 
the gravel beach and nearshore area. A dive inspection completed in June 2020 noted that the 
shallow waters offshore contain rubble material of a variety of sizes up to approximately a 1m diameter.  This material does not extend very far offshore and that the lakebed quickly changes 
to consist of fine sand only. The slope offshore was noted as very gradual. A  collection of armour 
stones is scattered at the southern end of beach where a park’s pond outlets into the lake. These 
armour stones form the remnants of a protection structure for the creek outlet that has failed.  
This report discusses the condition and state of protection structures for the entire project area. 
Phase 1 of the detailed design drawings focuses on the protection structures for the northern 
headland.  On our design drawings, this area is represented by chainage as between stations 
0+550m and approximately 1+220m. Station 1+220m is located at the northern tip of the northern 
headland. The small beach to the southwest of this point is not part of the scope of this project . A 
multi- use tr ail runs along the entire shoreline of the northern headland. Some sections of the trail 
have been undermined due to the ongoing erosion.  
The shoreline is currently protected by  armour stone revetment structures.  The size of the armour 
stones , the crest elevations and the nature of placement of the armour stones varies considerably.  
The armour stone sizes are most commonly in the 2 to 4 tonne range but larger stones up to 6 tonnes, and some even larger ones, were found on the site. High water levels experienced 
between 2017 and 2019 have led to wave overtopping of the revetments  causing erosion behind 
the revetment structures , as well as erosion behind the beach area and loss of beach material . 
As a result, armour stones have shifted , slid down  or fallen bac k (landward) , and lowered  the 
elevation of the structure, further reducing the efficacy of the protection structures.  Our 
observations indicate that most of the damage is in areas  with lower crest elevations and 
specifically in areas of lower crest elevation that are exposed to higher waves.  The wave 
distribution around the site is discussed in the next section.
