3 Coastal  Assessment   
3.1 Water Levels  
Marine structures on the Canadian shores of the Great Lakes are typically designed using the 
100-year return period instantaneous water level for the design water level. The 100- year 
instantaneous water level commonly used in the Toronto area over the last few decades was 
75.7m above the Canadian Geodetic Vertical Datum of 1928 (CGVD28). That value was 
determined by MNR (1989), but record high Lake Ontario water levels in 2017 and again in 2019 
have led to the reconsideration of design water levels.  
3.1.1  Extreme Value Analysis 
We completed new extreme value analyses of annual maxima hourly water levels measured at the Canadian Hydrographic Service water level gauge in Toronto Harbour, for the 58 -year period 
from 1962- 2019.  That covers the post -regulation period for Lake Ontario, as discussed in Section 
3.1.3 , below . Four probability distributions were considered (Gumbel, Weibull, Log Pearson- 3, 
and GEV) with Gumbel distribution providing the best fit to the data.  Figure 3-1 shows the results 
of the Gumbel analysis.  
TRCA’s planning department has moved towards adopting a design water level of 76.2m 
IGLD1985 . Figure 3-1 shows that elevation corresponds to the 100 -year return period water level 
at approximately the upper 70% confidence interval  from our analysis.  A project team decision 
was made to use that same design water level for this project in order to be consistent  within 
TRCA’s different departments. A water level of 76.2 m IGLD1985 corresponds to 76 .15 m 
CGVD28. 
Figure 3-1 Toronto Water Level Extreme Value Analysis Results

3.1.2  Climate Change  
A considerable amount of research has been done on climate change and its expected effects on 
the Great Lakes  and while results vary considerably, there is general consensus on several key 
points. Overall, water levels are expected to fall while severe storm frequency and intensity are both expected to increase. Climate change impacts on Lake Ontario water levels are expected to be less than on the other Great Lakes because its water levels are regulated.  
McDermid et al. (2015) synthesized available science on the observed and predicted impacts of climate change in the Great Lakes basin. They reported a lack of clarity in the understanding of 
multiple factors influencing water level projections for the Great Lakes, and a low confidence in 
the current projections of future water levels  resulting from climate change. Given the low 
confidence in predicted future water levels we have not included specific climate change 
considerations in the assessment of design water levels.  
The extent to which the 2017 and 2019 high water levels can be attributed to climate change has 
been subject to some debate. Changes to Lake Ontario’s regulation plan, implemented in January 2017, may have contributed to the 2017 record high water level. High precipitation levels in Lake Ontario’s watershed was the predominant cause of the high lake levels, but the extent to which that may be directly attributed to climate change has not been precisely quantified. Most available 
models suggest that Lake Ontario water levels will go down, but there is too much uncertainty in 
the current research.  
3.1.3  IJC Plan 2014  
The International Joint Commission (IJC) began regulating Lake Ontario water level in 1960 but 
soon re- evaluated their plans due to low water levels at Montreal. Plan 1958- D was implemented 
in October 1963 and was in effect until Plan 2014 was implemented in January 2017. Baird (2019) 
describes quarter -monthly Lake Ontario water levels simulations under Plan 1985DD and Plan 
2014 from 1900 to 2008, completed by Environment Canada. They recommended a 7cm increase in the design mean water level due to the expected impacts of the implementation of Plan 2014. 
The simulation results predicted that implementing Plan 2014 would lead to different increases in 
mean water levels, depending upon the return period considered. The increase at the 100- year 
return period was 0.07 metres.  
There is some uncertainty about how this 0.07m difference should be considered as part of an updated 100 -year instantaneous water level within the LOSH. Plan 2014 was in use when the 
2017 and 2019 high water levels occurred so its effect on those levels, w hatever it may have 
been, are included in the updated extreme value analysis  described in 3.1.1 . The International 
Joint Commission has undertaken a review of Plan 2014 and has acknowledged the possibility that it could be changed, if required.  
In the absence of clear evidence regarding the impact of Plan 2014 on the 2017 and 2019 high water levels, and considering that IJC may change the plan, we have not considered changes due to the plan in our assessment of design water levels. It is worth noting, however, that our design assessment is based on the 70% confidence interval of the extreme value analysis, which is approximately 13cm higher than the esti mated 100- year return period water level.

3.2 Bathymetry  
Bathymetric data was used in both the numerical and physical models. TRCA provided site 
bathymetry. That was merged with the most current Canadian Hydrographic Service (CHS) field 
sheet data to produce the project bathymetry. Figure 3-2 is a contour map showing the project 
bathymetry.

3.3 Wave Hindcasting  
The hindcast  was used to provide hourly estimates of significant wave height, peak wave period 
and mean wave direction for the 46- year period from January  1, 1973 to December  31, 2018. The  
wave climate has a bi -nodal distribution with wave height and wave energy peaks in the easterly 
and southwesterly directions, as shown in Figure 3-3. Although the highest easterly wave is 
similar in height to the highest southwesterly wave, there are a significant number of higher waves 
coming from the east than the southwest. This can be seen from the wave height exceedance curves in Figure 3-4, which show the percentage of time any given significant wave height was 
exceeded during the 47- year hindcast.

3.3.1  Design Waves  
Peak -over-threshold extreme value analyses were completed for both easterly and southwesterly 
storm events. The upper 90% confidence interval for the 100- year return period estimates of 
significant wave height and peak wave period were 5. 6m and 9.8s for an easterly wave and 4.4 m 
8.5s for a southwesterl y wave.  Figure 3-5 and Figure 3-6 show the wave height contour and vector 
plot illustrating the significant wave heights at the site for the worst case directions, east and 
southwest winds. The maximum wave height in front of the shoreline was 3 .46 m and has a peak 
wave period of 10.5 seconds. These values were used for design of new coastal protection 
structures .

4 Shoreline Protection Detailed Design  
Shoreplan has evaluated the existing site  conditions as described in Section 2, and considered 
the coastal conditions along the shoreline described in Section 3 of this report.  
4.1 Conceptual Design Options  
On the basis of the assessment and conditions, two conceptual approaches to remediation were 
considered for the revetment portions of the shoreline. These approaches included, as option 1, 
complete rebuil d of the revetment using, as much as possible the existing armour stone and 
supplement with additional armour as required, and, as option 2, supplementing the existing 
structures with additional armour stone while leaving the existing structures in place. A decision 
to proceed with option 2 was made in consultation with TRCA staff.  The main advantages of 
option 2 is substantial reduc tion of overall disturbance of the shoreline and likely eliminated risk 
of expos ure of existing fill material and overall reduced construction cost and time. Option 2 
includes a short section of the shoreline at the east side of the central beach where a new revetment is  to be constructed.  
The beach stabilization options included a review of different size of beach materials  that could 
be used to supplement the existing beach profiles.  Details of the pond outlet structure located in 
the west part of the beach have been developed and are included as part of the beach sector. 
The outlet design is  site specific and involves a completely new construction.  
4.2 Phase 1 Detailed Design  
Detailed designs have been provided for all reaches of  the shoreline with improved details for 
backfill, backshore grading and repair to the multi -use trail along the shoreline.  
The design of the revetments specifically considered the crest elevation and the crest width giving 
considerations to the existing backshore elevations. The objective of the crest design was to 
ensure unobstructed view of the lake, reduce the extent of backfill in the backshore that would impact existing vegetation, particularly mature trees, and ensure that overtopping rates were low  
enough to control backshore damage. The overtopping rate was limited to 10 l/m/s.   
Details of our design can be seen on the design drawings 19 -3133 r2 PHASE 1 sheets 1 to 5. In 
this report, the design will be discussed in separate sections for the south facing shoreline of the headland (stations 0+ 625m to 0+ 800m), the southern tip (s tations 0+ 825m to 0+925m ), east 
facing shoreline (s tations 0+ 950m to 1+175m ), and shoreline along the northern tip of the 
headland (s tations 1+200m to end). The cross sections of these reaches can be seen on sheets 
2 to 4 of the design drawings respectively.  
 
4.2.1  Stations 0+ 625m to 0+ 800m 
For the southern facing shoreline of the northern headland, the existing armour stone revetment 
needs  repair and  enhancement, and in the western end of this reach, in need of new construction

due to the severity of erosion and lack of existing revetment structure. Design wave heights at 
this reach range from 2.2m to 2.6m.  
From stations 0+625 m to 0+ 800m the existing revetment structure is to remain in place and be 
supplemented with additional 2.0 -4.0 tonne armour stone placed at a 1.5H:1.0V slope.  The 
structure will rise to a crest elevation of 78.5m, where it will be topped with 3.0- 5.0m cap stones. 
The existing armour stones at the crest of the structure will be removed so the new cap stones 
can be founded on new rip rap. The width of the cap in various areas of this reach depends on the predicted wave overtopping of the structure, discussed in Section 4.2.5.1 . The backshore 
behind the structure will be raised with fill to an elevation of 77.75m, complete with topsoil and sod.  
4.2.2  Stations 0+ 825m to 0+925m 
Revetment structures are currently in place along the southern tip of the northern headland but 
have been damaged during recent high water levels and require repair and enhancement. Additional random placement armour stone, ranging from 2.0-4.0 tonne to 3.0-5.0 tonne 
dependant on the design wave height modelled at each location, will be placed along this reach 
of shoreline to enhance the existing revetment structures. The largest waves are  expected at the 
southern tip of the headland (0+82 0 to 0+925), as seen in Figure 3-5. The structure slope is 
changed to 2H:1V after station for 0+800m for increased stability of the armour stones. The crest 
elevation of the revetment structure will rise from the 78.5m elevation of the previous reach as the structure continues along the southern tip, reaching a maximum crest elevation of 79.5m 
between stations 0+850 and 0+900, where wave heights are modelled to be the highest. The 
crest elevation decreases gradually to 78.0 m as the structure progresses along the shoreline from 
the southern ti p towards the east facing shoreline. The crest of the structure along this reach 
features 3.0 -5.0 tonne cap stones underlain by 300- 600mm rip rap, ranging from 2 to 4m wide 
depending on expecting wave overtopping.  
4.2.3  Stations 0+950m to 1+ 175m 
Revetment structures are currently in place along the east facing shoreline of the northern headland but have been damaged during recent high water levels and require repair and 
enhancement. Additional random placement armour stone, ranging from 2 .0-4.0 tonne to 3.0 -5.0 
tonne dependant on the design wave height modelled at each location, will be placed along this 
reach of shoreline to enhance the existing revetment structures. The largest waves are expected 
at the northern tip of the headland (1+100m to 1+755m), as seen in Figure 3-5. The structure 
slope remains 2H:1V for increased stability of the armour stones. The crest elevation of the revetment structure is lowest between stations 0+975 and 1+025 (77.5m elevation) and gradually increases moving north as expected wave heights increase, reaching a maximum crest elevation of 78.5m at station 1+125, where wave heights are modelled to be the highest. The crest elevation decreases gradually to 77.8m as the structure progresses north along the shoreline. The crest of 
the structure along this reach features 3.0- 5.0 tonne cap stones underlain by 300- 600mm rip rap, 
ranging from 3 to 5m wide depending on expecting wave overtopping.

4.2.4  Stations 1+200m to end  
As the revetment structure wraps west around the northern tip of the headland, expected wave 
heights become less severe. Additional random placement 2.0 -4.0 tonne armour stones will be 
placed to repair and enhance existing protection. The crest of the structure along this reach 
features 3.0-5.0 tonne cap stones approximately 2m wide and placed to a crest elevation of 77.3. 
The cap stones will be underlain by 300 -600mm rip rap.  
4.2.5  Design Considerations and Construction Costs, Phase 1  
4.2.5.1  Wave Overtopping  
The overtopping rate was estimated using the methods for armoured slopes and walls presented in the Overtopping Manual (EurOtop, 2018). For the revetment structures, the overtopping rate is calculated at the back of the crest of the structure.  
The crest elevation and width of the structure was established so that the estimated wave 
overtopping during design conditions would be approximately 10 l/s/m, a small amount that will 
be mitigated by the rip rap splash pad behind the cap stones. According to the EurOtop manual 
(2018), this value will  create minimal damage to the backshore area.  
4.2.5.2  Effects on Coastal Processes 
The new revetment structures planned for Phase 1 of this project are placed on the same footprint 
as the existing revetment structures and therefore have a minimal impact on local coastal 
processes.  The new revetment structure placed between stations 0+550m and 0+650m will 
stabilize and prevent further erosion of the shoreline in that area. 
4.2.5.3  Stone Sizing  
Armour stone sizes were selected for stability using the Hudson equation and using the design 
coastal conditions detailed in Section 3 for design input.  
4.2.5.4  Cut and Fill Volumes   
Cutting into the bank was  minimized during the design process to avoid exposing potential 
contaminated material.  Additional fill material will be required behind the crest of the new 
revetment structures to tie into existing grades. Phase 1 will include , in addition to stone materials 
listed in section 4.3.9, approximately  3,300  cubic meters  of fill material and 900 cubic meters of 
topsoil .  
4.2.5.5  List of Assumptions & Limitations  
There are limitations in evaluating the existing revetment structures. The thickness and toe depth 
of the existing armour stone and rip rap layers cannot be understood without dismantling the 
structure with large equipment.  Minimum layer thicknesses and bottom elevations for the 
reinforced revetment designs have been specified and it will be the responsibility of the contractor to ensure that the final structure is built to specifications.

It was assumed that the native material composition of the shoreline had potential to be 
contaminated and therefore the protection structures were designed to minimize cutting and disturbing of native material.  
4.2.5.6  Aquatic Habitat Alteration & Loss  
The effect of proposed structure on aquatic habitat was evaluated in plan view to calculate  the 
areas of fish habitat that would be altered or lost as a result of the construction. The area of aquatic habitat lost, which is the area of the structure’s  footprint at the DFO high water mark (75.3 
m IGLD , 1985 ), is approximately 2,380 m
2. The area of altered habitat, measured as the structure 
footprint below elevation 75.3 m, would be approximately 2, 850m2.  
4.2.5.7  Vibrations from Construction  
Equipment to be used in the construction includes excavators, skid steers, and front end loaders. There is no requirement for equipment that would generate an excessive amount of  vibration that 
would propagate beyond the construction area and cause any adverse effects.  
4.2.5.8  Vegetation Removal and Protection  
The proposed structure was designed to have minimal impact on local vegetation. Trees located at the crest of the existing shoreline where the new revetment structures are to be constructed 
will need to be removed or replaced. All other trees will be protected as part of the project.  
4.2.5.9  Volumes Estimate  
A construction materials  estimate was prepared for the design described above. The quantities 
were estimated using cross sectional areas of each design between stations 0+550m and 1+200m. The volumes were calculated by multiplying each cross- sectional  area by 25m, the 
spacing between cross sections. The estimates are only for new materials to be added and do not include existing armour stone on site. A summary of the estimated tonnages  is provided in  
Table 4-1. 
The order of magnitude construction cost estimate is for the works proposed in Phase 1 is 
estimated to be $5, 500,000.  This amount does not include any contingencies or taxes.

The objective of the crest design was to ensure unobstructed view of the lake, reduce the extent of backfill in the backshore that would impact existing vegetation, particularly mature trees, and ensure that overtopping rates were low 
enough to control backshore damage. The overtopping rate was limited to 10 l/m/s.

drawings respectively.

area to preserve  tree and minimize
damage to trunk where possible.

of 5Drawing Location: S:\Shoreplan Project Files\Files 3100-3199\19-3133 Humber Bay Park East TRCA\Drawings\19-3133 r7 PHASE 1.dwgShore plan Engineering Limited
4C.V.GENERAL CONSTRUCTION NOTES:
SECTION 1:  SITE MANAGEMENT
1) Erosion  and sediment  control  (ESC)  measures  will be implemented  prior to, and maintained  during  the construction  phases,  to prevent  entry  of sediment  into the water.  All damaged  erosion  and sediment  control  measures  should  be
repaired and/or replaced within 48 hours of the inspection.
2) Disturbed areas will be minimized to the extent possible, and temporarily or permanently stabilized or restored as the work progresses.
3) The erosion  and sediment  control  strategies  outlined  on the plans  are not static  and may need  to be upgraded/amended  as site conditions  change  to minimize  sediment  laden  runoff  from leaving  the work  areas.  If the prescribed
measures  on the plans  are not effective  in preventing  the release  of deleterious  substance,  including  sediment,  then alternative  measures  must  be implemented  immediately  to minimize  potential  ecological  impacts.  TRCA  enforcement
officer should be immediately contacted. Additional ESC measures to be kept on site and used as necessary.
4) All activities,  including  maintenance  procedures,  will be controlled  to prevent  the entry  of petroleum  products,  debris,  rubble,  concrete  or other  deleterious  substances  into the water.  Vehicular  refueling  and maintenance  will be conducted
a minimum 30 metres from the water.
5) All grades within the regulatory flood plain will be maintained or matched unless otherwise specified.
6) The proponent  / contractor  shall monitor  the weather  several  days  in advance  of the onset  of the project  to ensure  that the works  will be conducted  during  favorable  weather  conditions.  Should  an unexpected  storm  arise,  the contractor
will remove all unfixed items from the regional storm flood plain that would have the potential to cause a spill or an obstruction to flow (e.g. fuel tanks, porta-poptties, machinery, equipment, construction materials, etc.).
SECTION 2:  CONSTRUCTION TIMING
7) In order to comply with the migratory birds convention act, TRCA recommends that tree removals be completed between August 1 and April 1.
8) To protect local fish populations during their spawning, nursery, and migratory periods, fish monitoring will occur from June 1 to July 15. This will
determine the presence of spawning Emerald Shiner, which have been recorded in the area. If any are found, in water work will cease.
SPECIFICATIONS:
1.Work  includes  preparation  of the access  to the site, demolition  and/or  removal  of items  indicated  on the contract  drawing  or required  to complete
the specified  work,  tree and brush  removal  required  to complete  the work,  removal  of existing  armour  stone  structures  and concrete  rubble  and
miscellaneous  other  site preparation  tasks  required  to complete  the specified  works,  disposal  of the derived  or excess  material  in a legal  manner,
and the restoration  of the sites and access  routes  to existing  or better  conditions.  Ashphalt  path to be restored  as per City of Toronto  standard
T-221.01. Erect any silt curtains or fences as required by conditions of approvals and comply with any and all conditions outlined on permits.
2.Riprap  stone  shall be hard,  dense,  angular,  quarry  stone,  free from seams,  cracks  or other  structural  defects  with relative  density  2.7, +/-0.15.
Riprap  material  shall be rock,  ranging  in size from 300 to 600 mm. Minimum  50% of the total volume  shall contain  stone  of individual  size greater
than 450 mm and not more  than 10% of volume  shall contain  stone  less than 350 mm in size. Specified  dimensions  represent  the smallest
dimension  of individual  rock pieces.  In case  of a dispute  regarding  the size of material  the contractor  shall provide  scientific  proof  of size at his
expense. Place riprap to lines and grades indicated on the contract drawings.
3.Armour  stone  for random  placement  shall be hard,  durable,  quarry  stone,  free from cracks,  seam  or other  defects  which  may impair  durability.
Individual  stones  in masses  varying  as indicated  on the contract  drawings.  Least  dimension  of each  stone  not less than 2/3 of greatest  dimension.
Relative  density  of 2.7, +/- 0.15.  The stones  shall be angular  or semi-angular.  Use of existing  armour  stones  is permitted  where  armour  stones
meet specified requirements.
4.Armour  stone  for cap and toe shall be hard,  durable,  quarry  stone,  free from cracks,  seam  or other  defects  which  may impair  durability.  Individual
stones  in masses  varying  as indicated  on the contract  drawings.  Least  dimension  of each  stone  not less than 2/3 of greatest  dimension.  Relative
density  of 2.7, +/- 0.15.  The stones  shall have  roughly  parallel  top and bottom  surfaces.  The stones  shall be angular  or semi-angular  and roughly
cubical  in shape.  Use of existing  armour  stones  is permitted  where  armour  stones  meet  specified  requirements.  Select  cap stone  and place  to
provide uniform horizontal and vertical alignment.
5.The filter fabric  material  (geotextile)  shall be non-woven  pervious,  inert and durable.  The material  shall be Terrafix  800R  or approved  equal.
Individual  filter fabric  sheets  shall reach  from the top of the revetment  to the toe. Provide  a minimum  of 800 mm overlap  width  in the across  the
slope  direction  when  placing  a new section  of filter fabric  adjacent  to a previously  placed  section.  Repair  any damaged  filter fabric  by placing
second  layer,  overlaying  the damaged  area by not less then 1500  mm in all directions  beyond  the damaged  area.  Use geotextile  under  rip rap
whenever fine grained soils are encountered.
6.  General fill material shall be clean native or imported soil material suitable for compaction. Use of sand or gravel from the existing shore is not to
be permitted. All fill material shall be compacted to minimum 98% S.P.D. All fill material to be rough graded and matched to existing grades to the
satisfaction of the owner. The fill shall be free draining soil material suitable for compaction and meets Ontario Regulation 153/4, for parkland use.
Use of Select Subgrade Material as per OPSS 1010 is also suitable.
7. Granular A as per OPSS 1010.
8. Seed mix as per City of Toronto TS 5.20, seed mix for upland meadows

1.All dimensions  in millimeters  unless
indicated otherwise.
2.All elevations in meters, GSC.
3.Soundings  and topographic  information
provided  by the TRCA.  Vertical  control
system  (GSC)  Horizontal  control  system
(NAD83)
4.Existing  stone  protection  work  shall be
supplemented  with the specifed  armour
stone  and/or  rip rap material.  The
placement  of new material  may require
adjustment  of existing  armour  stones  to
ensure  stability  and proper  interlock  of
existing  and new armour  stone  under
and below the new armour stone.
5.Slope  land side of rip rap at angle  of
repose.
6.Existing  rubble  and/or  armour  stone
located  lakeward  of the proposed
revetment  improvements  shall remain.
Any rubble  or armour  stone  removed
lakeward  of the proposed  improvements
shall be replaced  with equal  size
material at the same location.
7.Existing  concrete  rubble  found  on site
may be re-used  as rip rap material  if
appropriate  size. Concrete  material
should  be located  in the core of new
structure  and only new rip rap visible  in
splash pad.
8.Adjust  fill around  large  trees  within  fill
area to preserve  tree and minimize
damage to trunk where possible.

Tree Hoarding Area
see Detail TP-1XXGENERAL CONSTRUCTION NOTES:
SECTION 1:  SITE MANAGEMENT
1) Erosion and sediment control (ESC) measures will be implemented prior to, and maintained during the construction phases, to prevent entry of
sediment into the water. All damaged erosion and sediment control measures should be repaired and/or replaced within 48 hours of the inspection.
2) Disturbed areas will be minimized to the extent possible, and temporarily or permanently stabilized or restored as the work progresses.
3) The erosion and sediment control strategies outlined on the plans are not static and may need to be upgraded/amended as site conditions change
to minimize sediment laden runoff from leaving the work areas. If the prescribed measures on the plans are not effective in preventing the release
of deleterious substance, including sediment, then alternative measures must be implemented immediately to minimize potential ecological
impacts. TRCA enforcement officer should be immediately contacted. Additional ESC measures to be kept on site and used as necessary.
4) All activities, including maintenance procedures, will be controlled to prevent the entry of petroleum products, debris, rubble, concrete or other
deleterious substances into the water. Vehicular refueling and maintenance will be conducted a minimum 30 metres from the water.
5) All grades within the regulatory flood plain will be maintained or matched unless otherwise specified.
6) The proponent / contractor shall monitor the weather several days in advance of the onset of the project to ensure that the works will be conducted
during favorable weather conditions. Should an unexpected storm arise, the contractor will remove all unfixed items from the regional storm flood
plain that would have the potential to cause a spill or an obstruction to flow (e.g. fuel tanks, porta-poptties, machinery, equipment, construction
materials, etc.).
SECTION 2:  CONSTRUCTION TIMING
7) In order to comply with the migratory birds convention act, TRCA recommends that tree removals be completed between August 1 and April 1.
8) To protect local fish populations during their spawning, nursery, and migratory periods, fish monitoring will occur from June 1 to July 15. This will
determine the presence of spawning Emerald Shiner, which have been recorded in the area. If any are found, in water work will cease.
SPECIFICATIONS:
1. Work includes preparation of the access to the site, demolition and/or removal of items indicated on the contract drawing or required to complete the
specified work, tree and brush removal required to complete the work, removal of existing armour stone structures and concrete rubble and
miscellaneous other site preparation tasks required to complete the specified works, disposal of the derived or excess material in a legal manner, and
the restoration of the sites and access routes to existing or better conditions. Ashphalt path to be restored as per City of Toronto standard T-221.01.
Erect any silt curtains or fences as required by conditions of approvals and comply with any and all conditions outlined on permits.
2. Riprap stone shall be hard, dense, angular, quarry stone, free from seams, cracks or other structural defects with relative density 2.7, +/-0.15. Riprap
material shall be rock, ranging in size from 300 to 600 mm. Minimum 50% of the total volume shall contain stone of individual size greater than 450
mm and not more than 10% of volume shall contain stone less than 350 mm in size. Specified dimensions represent the smallest dimension of
individual rock pieces. In case of a dispute regarding the size of material the contractor shall provide scientific proof of size at his expense. Place riprap to lines and grades indicated on the contract drawings.
3. Armour stone for random placement shall be hard, durable, quarry stone, free from cracks, seam or other defects which may impair durability.
Individual stones in masses varying as indicated on the contract drawings. Least dimension of each stone not less than 2/3 of greatest dimension.
Relative density of 2.7, +/- 0.15. The stones shall be angular or semi-angular. Use of existing armour stones is permitted where armour stones meet
specified requirements.
4. Armour stone for cap and toe shall be hard, durable, quarry stone, free from cracks, seam or other defects which may impair durability. Individual
stones in masses varying as indicated on the contract drawings. Least dimension of each stone not less than 2/3 of greatest dimension. Relative density of 2.7, +/- 0.15. The stones shall have roughly parallel top and bottom surfaces. The stones shall be angular or semi-angular and roughly
cubical in shape. Use of existing armour stones is permitted where armour stones meet specified requirements. Select cap stone and place to provide
uniform horizontal and vertical alignment.
5. The filter fabric material (geotextile) shall be non-woven pervious, inert and durable. The material shall be Terrafix 800R or approved equal. Individual
filter fabric sheets shall reach from the top of the revetment to the toe. Provide a minimum of 800 mm overlap width in the across the slope direction
when placing a new section of filter fabric adjacent to a previously placed section. Repair any damaged filter fabric by placing second layer, overlaying
the damaged area by not less then 1500 mm in all directions beyond the damaged area. Use geotextile under rip rap whenever fine grained soils are
encountered.
6.  General fill material shall be clean native or imported soil material suitable for compaction. Use of sand or gravel from the existing shore is not to be
permitted. All fill material shall be compacted to minimum 98% S.P.D. All fill material to be rough graded and matched to existing grades to the
satisfaction of the owner. The fill shall be free draining soil material suitable for compaction and meets Ontario Regulation 153/4, for parkland use.
Use of Select Subgrade Material as per OPSS 1010 is also suitable.
7. Granular A as per OPSS 1010.
8. Seed mix as per City of Toronto TS 5.20, seed mix for upland meadows

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    1 1 INTRODUCTION  
 Project Background  
Humber Bay Park is a waterfront park  that is located at the mouth of Mimico Creek  on the north shore of Lake 
Ontario  in Etobicoke, City of Toronto . The park consists of two peninsulas  (Humber Bay Park  East and Humber Bay 
Park West ) that extend  out into Lake Ontario.  Toronto and Region Conservation Authority (TRCA) owns the land 
the park is built on and the City of Toronto undertakes the maintenance and administration of the park.  Park 
construction began in the 1970s  as a lake -filling operation which reached its curre nt form in the 1990s . The 
parklands are armoured with a series of headlands and beaches  to protect the shoreline from erosion. The park 
is a large urban green space, featuring wetlands, forests, and meadows, as well as several cultural and natural 
heritage  features . The park is heavily utilized by the local community ; this usage is expected to increase as the 
neighbourhood continues to evolve into a dense, high -rise residential community.  
The Project will focus on the eastern peninsula of Humber Bay Park, which is protected by six TRCA -owned erosion 
control structures along the Lake Ontario shoreline. These structures were built in the early 1980s and are in 
various states of disrepair. TRCA’s  Erosion Risk Management Program (ERMP)  has monitored the  park  structures  
on an annual basis  since 2006.  Based on the monitoring records, three of the six structures have been identified 
as priorities for repair and are included in the scope of work.  Both t he western armourstone headland (WF 11.10) 
and the  rubble /gravel beach (WF11.11) are currently  classified as in overall “fair” condition, but with a structural 
stability rating of “failing” due to several deficiencies . The eastern armourstone headland  (WF11.12) is classified 
as in overall “poor” condition with a structural stability rating of “failing”  due to its deficiencies .  
In the fall of 2016, Riggs Engineering Limited undertook a comprehensive assessment of the 73 TRCA -owned 
shoreline erosion control structures between Marie Curtis Park in the west and the western extent of Ontario 
Place in the east. Based on the ranking system accounting for structure condition, adjacent land use function, 
environmental factors, and others, Humber Bay Park  (East and West ) ranked fifth  overall in priority  for repairs . 
Since this comprehensive assessment took place the condition  of these structures has further deteriorated due to  
the 2017 and 2019  high lake level events as well as the  April 14 -15, 2018  wind storm. Major maintenance is 
required to return these structures to a s tate of good repair and provide long- term protection to the Humber Bay 
Park East shoreline.

2 SITE DESCRIPTION  
 Location  
The site is located in Humber Bay Park East , along the north shore of  Lake Ontario in  Etobicoke, City of Toronto . 
The closest major intersection is  Lake Shore Boulevard West and Park Lawn Road  with t he park accessible via 
Marine Pa rade  Drive . The general location of the site and erosion control structures are shown below in Figure 2.  

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    3  
Figure 2. Map of Hu mber Bay Park East showing the project location . Source: TRCA, 2019.  
 Land Usage  
Humber Bay Park East  is a public p ark owned by TRCA. The park has formal and informal trails  that run parallel to 
the shoreline  and throughout the park, a publicly accessible beach , and the Air India 182 Memorial.  The park 
features a system of streams and ponds, one of which drains at the eastern limit of the western headland 
(WF11.10).  
 Structure  Description  
2.3.1 Phase I –  Eastern Armourstone Headland (WF11.12)  
The eastern armourstone headland (WF11.12) , seen in Figure 3, has a structural stability rating of “ failing” and an 
overall condition of “ poor” as evaluated in ERMP inspections since 2018 . The structure has experienced 
displacement of stone material across its entire length, including toe, slope, and crest stone  as shown  in the ERMP 
sketch  below  (Figure 4). As a result, the structure has an irregular crest  profile  and several large scours are present 

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    4 along the backshore.  This irregular profile places low lying areas at greater risk of flooding  during elevated lake 
levels.  Additional stone has been placed in many of the scours  to temporarily improve shoreline protection ( Figure 
5 and Figure 6), however, these are interim measures only . The western limit of the structure near the transition 
to the beach  has experienced complete material displacement and significant scouring resultin g in damage to a 
heavily utilized informal park  trail and presenting a risk to park users  (Figure 7 and Figure 8).

 ANSI/ESA Areas  
There are no Areas of Natural or Scientific Importance (ANSI s) or Environmentally Sensitive Areas  (ESAs) located 
within the project area. The closest ANSI/ESA is the Humber River  which is  750 m away  and therefore falls 
outside  the project area.  
 Typical Slope Dimensions  
The topography of the eastern headland  is shown in Figure 17. At the western limit of the structure near the beach 
the crest of the headland sits around the 76 metres above- sea-level (m.a.s.l.) and the  parkland behind the 
structure  sits between 76 and 77 m.a.s.l. The typical elevation of the crest of the rest of  the structure sits around 
76 m.a.s.l. and the parkland behind the structure  sits between 77 and 78 m.a.s.l.  The typical near shore depth is 
approximately 72 -73 m.a.s.l.

 Archaeological Assessment  
In 2017- 2018, TRCA Archaeology Staff conducted a Stage 1 Archaeological Assessment in support of the Western 
Waterfront Major Mainte nance Strategy. The scope of this assessment encompassed the Humber Bay Park East 
Shoreline  Maintenance Project Area. The Stage 1  assessment determined that Humber Bay Park East has no 
archaeological potential due to the fact that is a lakefill park that w as constructed in the 1970s and 1980s.

 Species At Risk  
A Species at Risk (SAR) screening was conducted by TRCA staff. The following SAR have been recorded within the 
project vicinity : 
• American eel ( Anguilla rostrata)  
• Barn swallow ( Hirundo rustica)  
• Black snakeroot ( Actaea racemosa)  
• Eastern wood pewee ( Contopus virens ) 
• Northern map turtle ( Graptemys geographica)  
• Old field toad flax ( Nuttallanthus canadensis ) 
 
TRCA Fauna Biologist , Paul Prior,  was asked to comment on the fauna species identified during the screening. He 
indicated that the habitat requirement for the barn swallow and eastern wood pewee are not present at the 
Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    19 project site. B arn swallow s nest in man -made structures  such as under b ridges  and the park’s habitat is unsuitable 
for the eastern wood pewee . Despite this it was advised that construction begin in outside of the breeding window  
(early April to late August ) to ensure that there is no disruption to nesting activities. The northern map turtle is 
unlikely to nest in a park with high levels of human disturbance as there are more choice habitats within the 
Humber River.  
TRCA Flora Biologist , Gavin Miller, was asked to comment about the two observed plant species : old field toad 
flax and black snakeroot. These species are no longer present in the park as of  the latest inventory conducted in 
2014.  
TRCA Fisheries Biologist , Brian Graham , indicated that there have been no historical American eels caught during 
fisheries monitoring along the open shore side of Humber Bay Park. American eels have only been discovered in 
the sheltered bays of the area. American eels are also transient in nature  therefore shoreline works are expected 
to have no impact on them. Eels travel along the shoreli ne to find suitable substrate and would simply move on if 
encountering a work area. As  predatory fish , they will follow their prey . Once the project has been completed, the 
substrate that will have been placed  for shoreline erosion (rocks with interstitial spaces ) will provide  favo urable  
conditions  for eel s. 
Because of the timing of the project, as well as the nature of the species in the Project area, there are no SAR 
concerns for undertaking shoreline works.  
 Soil Characterization  
For the Humber Bay Park East Revitalization Project  undertaken by TRCA’s Restoration and Resource Management 
Group  (RRM ), WSP Canada (WSP)  was retained to conduct a risk assessment  of the soil contamination within 
Humber Bay Park East. WSP generated three reports in 2020 based on  the findings: a Soil Management Plan, a 
Health and Safety Plan, and a Due Diligence Risk Assessment  Report.  It was discovered that the soils within the 
Revitalization  Project Area contained many contaminants  that are potentially Health and Safety risks for staff 
working on -site. As th is risk assessment was undertaken outside of the Shoreline Project area, the ERM team 
undert ook laboratory testing of surface soil along the shoreline of the Phase I project area.  The testing and analysis 
were completed in  Summer 2021 . Ten hand -dug samples were taken 0.0 to 0.4 m below the ground surface and 
tested for the presence of contaminants. The samples exceeded regulation for several metals (including lead, 
arsenic,  and ca dmium) as well as various polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons. However, the contamination levels 
were not elevated enough to consider the soils to be hazardous and they have been classified as non -hazardous 
under O.Reg 347/90. The results of this analysis will be considered as Health and Safety Plans are developed .

Digging will not be required in the area identified in Figure 19 however as this area will be heavily used by delivery 
trucks,  a pre and post construction CCTV inspection will be completed . Underground infra structure protection 
measures will be implemented in any areas where digging is required above or near buried infrastructure . Site 
access routes and staging areas will be set up to exclude above -ground infrastructure.  
6 SCOPE OF WORK  
The following scope of work will be completed as part of major maintenance for Phase I  to ensure long- term 
protection of the shoreline in the project area.  
 Construction Phasing  
The scope of work for Phase I will include the items listed below  and will completed in the following general 
sequence:  

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    23 1. Site setup including e stablish ment of  staging/stockpiling areas, remov al of required trees, re location 
of bird nesting boxes within the staging area, and installation of ESC measures, tree protection, and 
site perimeter control  
2. Construct ion of a  temporary  culvert and access bridge near the pond outlet 
3. Limited regrading of native fill material to accommodate repair works  and establishment  of 
construction road where the crest is too narrow on west side  
4. Repair of the eastern and southern ext ent of the headland  will occur in  10m from increments  from 
east to west. This will include:  
a. Removal and stockpiling of crest stone  and backshore excavation  as necessary  
b. Placement of habitat stone where appropriate  
c. Placement of  imported and stockpiled  armourstone (size range varies by station) in a random 
placement configuration along the slope of  the existing structure. S ome repositioning of the 
existing stone may be necessary to ensure stability . 
d. Installation of a geotextile base and 300-600 mm  rip rap along crest  
e. Installation of 3 -5 tonne cap stone along crest  in a special placement configuration  
5. Backshore regrading  and preliminary restoration (topsoil and seeding)  
6. Full s ite restoration, including compensation plantings and restoration of path features to return the 
area to a safe and functional state , to be completed following the implementation of all three phases . 
Portions of the access route and staging will be maintained for subsequent phases . 
 Detailed Scop e of Work  
The overall approach to the designs was to prioritize long -term resiliency while reducing the need for excavation 
and off -site disposal to limit disturbance and impacts of the construction activities  as well as addressing the 
leading erosion conc erns of the park, including low crest elevation and wave overtopping. The desig ns address 
these concerns by placing new material over the existing structures where toe and slope stability permits  thereby 
minimizing excavation requirements. The designs  were  developed using a  100-year design water level of 76.2  
m.a.s.l , a level chosen based on recommendations from a review of the effects of the 2017 and 2019 high lake 
level events on the nearby Toronto Islands conducted by W.F. Baird and Associates . The struc ture is designed to 
ensure the overtopping rate is below 10 litres/second.  
The treatment for most of the headland (stations 0+625 to 1+225) includes placement of new armourstone over 
the existing profile in order to elevate  the shore as shown in  the plan views in  Figure 20 and Figure 21. The slope 
of the  revetment structure will consist of  randomly placed armourstone of various size ranges built at a slope of 
2h:1v ratio.  Some minor repositioning of the existing structure may be necessary to ensure stability.  The size 
rang es vary by the level of protection required as determined by the condition of the structure and the results of 
the coastal analysis  however will generally be either 1 -2, 2-4, or 3-5 tonne armour stones . 
The crest elevation will be built up to a range of 77.5 to 79.5 m.a.s.l. with a width that ranges from 2 -5 m wide. 
The existing armourstone s along the crest will be removed  and stockpiled and some minor excavation of the 
backshore will be required. All stockpiled and excavated material will be reused onsit e. The crest will be 
constructed using a 300 -600 mm underlayer of rip rap overtop of a geotextile baselayer and capped with a single 
layer of 3 -5 tonne armourstone in a special placement configuration. The backshore and the adjacent trail will be 
Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    24 raised to  an elevation ranging from 77 to 79 m.a.s.l. using Granular B and capped with compacted topsoil. The 
shoreward side of the trail will have a slope of 1% with the parkward side of the trail will have a 5% slope for 
drainage. The cross -sections are shown in Figure 22. 
 
Figure 20. Planview of the southern extent of WF11.12. The yellow line represents  the sample cross section seen in Figure 
22. Source: Shoreplan, 2021.

 Aquatic Habitat Components  
Discussions are underway between TRCA, DFO, City of Toronto , and Shoreplan regarding the development of 
aquatic habitat features  for the design.  The proposed habitat component consists of a surcharged revetment in 
which additional stone material will be placed at the toe across portions of the proposed armourstone revetment 
in order to provide aquatic habitat ( Figure 23). Stone  will range in size from 100 -600 mm; material on the high 
end of this range will be cast beyond the toe in order to hold smaller material in place that will be cast along both 
the lower slope and toe of the revetment. The material will be placed in a random pattern in strategic locations 
across portions of the revetment length. Optimal placement will be confirmed in -field based on existing conditions, 
focusing on areas where existing offshore material does not already exist while also maintaining navigation depth 
requirements. This methodology is meant to enhance pre -existing offshore habitat pockets that are believed to 
have developed as a result of material washout from the previous structure failure. Therefore the exact placement 
of material across the structure length and slope will  differ from the representative visual provided in the plan 
view drawing  (Figure 20). Due to strong wave conditions modeled in the project area, material may naturally shift 
and resettle overtime. The transitory nature of the material negates the capacity to directly monitor physical 
structure form or characteristics.  
 
Figure 23. An example of surcharge stone at the toe of a revetment to act as aquatic habitat. Source: TRCA 2019.  
7 SURVEY REQUIREMENTS 
A layout survey will be conducted prior to the beginning of implementation and as needed throughout the project 
to facilitate construction. As -built surveys may be required th roughout the implementation process to verify the 
positioning of placed material. An as-built survey of the entire structure will be carried out upon completion of 
the work . 
8 CONSTRUCTION ACCESS, STAGING AND STOCKPILING  
The site will be accessed from Humber Bay Park Road East , located off Marine Parade Drive . Trucks will exit the 
road onto an informal trail via the access point shown below in Figure 24. Trucks will drive southeast to the first 
staging and stockpiling area on the west side of the site and continue  east along the beach , first crossing a 
constructed berm with a culvert, then rejoining the trail that parallels the coast until reaching the second staging 
area. The temporary culvert will be installed to allow for passage of trucks along the shoreline while still 

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    28 accommodating the drainage requirements for the ponds in the interior of the park.  During high wind and wave 
conditions trucks will deposit materials at the western staging area.  
 
Figure 24. Site access, staging and stockpiling areas and erosion and sediment control for Phase I.  Source: TRCA, 2021.  
 Required Tree Removals  
As part of the tree inventory , all trees above 10 cm diameter at breast h eight (DBH) as well as memorial or recently 
planted trees  were measured and tagged.  Any trees that require removal or will be potentially injured during the 
works were identified and are listed in Section 3.3 of the Arborist Report.  Tree removals will be kept to a minimum 
and be in accordance with TRCA policies. In order to complete the works, 80 trees and 87 whips will need to be 
removed to facilitate site access and construction activities. An additional 13 trees are listed as potential injuries 
based on TPZ encroachment and/or pruning needs.  Further discussion of compensation requirem ents is provided 
in Section 13.1 . 
 Hazardous /Stinging  Plants Screening  
No hazardous  or stinging  plants were  noted on site  by TRCA Surveying staff or during tree inventories completed 
by Forestry Services . Site conditions may have changed since the screening was completed and Construction staff 
should undertake a site -specific workplace hazard assessment of hazardous plants.  

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    29 9 EROSION A ND SEDIMENT CONTROL MEASURES  
Prior to the start of construction, staging and stockpiling areas  will be isolated with standard silt socks  to prevent 
the entry of deleterious substances into the watercourse, as shown previously Figure 24. All barriers will be 
inspected and maintained in proper working order throughout the duration of construction and will remain in 
place until the site is sufficiently stabilized.  
All waste materials will be removed from the work site to prevent them from entering the lake. 
At the end of each working day exposed soils shall be stabilized with appropriate erosion control matting where 
appropriate. Construction should be phased in a manner that limits the size of disturbed areas and minimizes 
clearing/grading. In general, excav ation should be limited to an area that can be stabilized by the end of the day 
to reduce the risk of leaving soil exposed for any prolonged duration. All disturbed areas shall be stabilized as soon 
as possible following construction and restored as described in Section 13. 
10 MEASURES TO PROTECT FISH AND FISH HABITAT  
The following measures will be used to protect any fish and their habitat : 
• Existing trails, roads, and cut lines will be used wherever possible to avoid excess disturbance of riparian 
vegetation.  
• The removal of existing vegetation will be kept to an absolute minimum.  
• Staff will minimize the required works within the watercourse and along its b anks. Construction shall proceed 
in a continuous fashion to minimize the duration of work.  
• Aquatic monitoring will be undertake n to ensure in -water works do not impact spawning fish  where necessary.  
• As stone will be used during construction of the in -wate r structures , the following measures will be 
incorporated to protect fish habitat:  
o Appropriately sized, clean rock will be placed to stabilize the area of concern.  
o Rocks will not be obtained from below the ordinary high -water mark of any water body.  
o Rock t hat is acid generated will be avoided  as will rock that fr actures and breaks down quickly when 
exposed to the elements.  
o Machinery will be kept in clean condition and in good working order to prevent the entry of any 
deleterious substances into the lake. Machinery shall be refueled no less than 30 metres from the lake.  
11 CONSTRUCTION BEST MANAGEMENT PRACTICES  
The proposed construction phasing sequence, as discussed in Section  6, recognizes site constraints, access routes, 
staging , storage requirements, and construction timing windows to facilitate the approved activities. The following 
best management practices (BMPs) will be employed during constr uction to ensure the ongoing protection of the 
environment and public safety:  
• A spill kit shall be kept on site at all times in the event of a spill resulting from machine breakdown or damage.  
Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase I –  Eastern Headland  
Project Brief  
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    30 • Tree protection barriers will be erected where necessary and wi ll be void of any grade changes and storage of 
materials/equipment within the Tree Protection Zone (TPZ).  
• All disturbed areas are to be restored to existing conditions or better. Any disturbed areas will be vegetated 
by planting and seeding with native tre es, shrubs , and grasses to prevent erosion. In the event that there is 
insufficient time remaining in the growing season, the site will be stabilized e.g., cover exposed areas with 
erosion control blankets to keep the soil in place and prevent erosion , and the plantings will be completed 
during the next appropriate planting window. In accordance with this, effective erosion and sediment control 
measures will be maintained until site is re -vegetated.  
• Machinery and vehicles will be kept clean of debris and se eds in order to prevent the spread of invasive species.  
12 CONSTRUCTION TIMING  
Construction of Phase I will start at the eastern limits of the armourstone headland  and proceed westward . 
Construction of Phase I is tentatively scheduled to start in mid -November 2021, pending the receipt of all required 
permits and approvals. Phase I  is anticipated to take 18 months in total, with completion by Spring 2023. Following 
completion of Phase I , construction will immediately proceed to Phase II and III. Opportunities for the concurrent 
implementation of future phases are currently being investigated to reduce the overall project time frame and 
return the site for public use as early as possible. Further details on the du ration of Phase II and III, including 
concurrent implementation strategies, will be determined as work proceeds .  
The Revitalization Project is a separate initiative from the shoreline maintenance works however both projects 
have been scheduled for a similar implementation start date and will run concurrently to  coordinate efforts and 
find efficiencies where possible. A  coordinated approach will be taken between the projects with regards to public 
communications and messaging.  
Phase I construction  will proceed through out the DFO  restricted activity timing window. Efforts will be ta ken to 
prioritize in -water works  outside of these windows, but due to the scale of the project, it is likely that i n-water 
works w ithin the fall and spring windows are likely to o ccur. Monitoring data shows that there are three species 
of fall spawning fish and four species of spring spawning fish. The three fall- spawning fish  – Atlantic Salmon, 
Chinook Salmon , and Brown Trout  – use river systems as spawning grounds  therefore construction activities would 
not interfere  with these species.  Four species of fish sampled during monitoring tend to spawn in lakes. Of these 
four species, Rainbow Smelt and Alewives  are not native to the area, Logperch spawn in areas absent o f swift 
currents  and may avoid the waves of the open coast , and the Emerald Shiner typically spawn in June when waters 
are warmer. Based on monitoring records, T RCA staff recommend weekly sampling be done  from  early  June to 
mid-July 2022  and if any spawnin g fish are detected, then in -water works will cease.  
Existing b ird nesting boxes  at are located within the identified staging area of Phase I.  The boxes will be relocated 
the beginning of project implementation in November of 2021. This is  outside of the breeding bird timing window 
(early April to late August ) and will ensure that  nesting activit ies are not impacted  by construction activity . Suitable  
spots in the park for relocation will be discussed with ecology staff.

As part of Phase I , 327 compensation plantings of at least 50 mm caliper are proposed to address the project 
impacts. Compensation will include both on and off -site plantings to accommodate site constraints and space 
limitations. The planting plan for Phase I includes 27 7 on-site plantings and 50 off -site plantings on City of Toronto 
parkland in Reid Manor Park.  The on -site plantings will consist of at least 30% eastern cottonwood  due to their 
fast-growing nature, allowing vegetated areas to develop while slower- growing trees are still developing . The rest 
of the planting species  will conform to the list in Section 7 of the Arborist Repot. This plan is preliminary and will 
be updated as subsequent phases of the project are completed. P roposed planting site locations  are shown in

 1 INTRODUCTION  
 Project Background  
Humber Bay Park is a waterfront park  that is located at the mouth  of Mimico Creek  on the north shore of Lake 
Ontario  in Etobicoke, City of Toronto . The park consists of two peninsulas  (Humber Bay Park E ast and Humber 
Bay Park West ) that extend  out into Lake Ontario.  Park construction began in the 1970s  as a lake -filling 
operation which reached its current form in the 1990s . The parklands are armoured w ith a series of headlands 
and beaches  to protect the shoreline from erosion. The park is a large urban green space, featuring wetlands, 
forests, and meadows, as well as several  cultural and natural heritage features . The park is heavily utilized by the 
local community ; this usage is expected to increase as the neighbourhood continues to evolve into a dense, 
high -rise residential community.  
The Project will focus on the eastern peninsula of Humber Bay Park, which is protected by six TRCA -owned 
erosion control structures along the Lake Ontario shoreline. These structures were built in the early 1980s and 
are in various states of disrepair. TRCA’s  Erosion Risk Management Program (ERMP)  has monitored the  park  
structures  on an annual basis  since 2006.  Based on the monitoring records, three of the six structures have been 
identified as priorities for repair and are included in the scope of work.  Both t he western armourstone headland 
(WF11.10) and the  rubble /gravel beach (WF11.11) are currently  classified  as in overall “fair” condition, but with 
a structural stability rating of “failing” due to several deficiencies . The eastern armourstone headland  (WF11.12) 
is classified as in overall “poor” condition with a structural stability rating of “failing”  due to  its deficiencies .  
In the fall of 2016, Riggs Engineering Limited undertook a comprehensive assessment of the 73 TRCA -owned 
shoreline erosion control structures between Marie Curtis Park in the west and the western extent of Ontario 
Place in the east. Bas ed on the ranking system , which accounted  for structure condition, adjacent land use 
function, environmental factors, and others, Humber Bay Park  (East and West ) ranked fifth  overall in priority  for 
repairs . Since this comprehensive assessment took place , the condition  of these structures has further 
deteriorated due to  the 2017 and 2019  high lake level events as well as the  April 14 -15, 2018  wind storm. Major 
maintenance is required to return these structures to a state of good repai r and provide long -term protection to 
the Humber Bay Park East shoreline.   
 Phasing  
Due to the extent of t he project area and  the multi -year nature of construction,  the Project has been divided 
into three phases for design and implementation:   PROJECT NAME  Humber Bay Park East Shoreline  Maintenance Project

 • Phase I : East ern Armourstone  Headland (W F11.12)  – repairs include the addition of  material over the 
existing structure  to raise it to a higher elevation  and widen the  crest.  
• Phase II : Western  Armourstone Headland  (WF11.10)  – repairs include the addition of material over the 
existing structure to raise it to a higher elevation and widen the crest . Work will also include 
formalization of the pond outlet , located  at the structures eastern transition to the beach , through the 
installation of an armourstone berm and plug. This phase also includes the transition point between the 
eastern headland and  beach.   
• Phase III : Concrete  Rubble /Gravel  Beach (WF11.11) – involves the  placement of cobble material  over the 
current beach profile.  
 
Phase I repairs were initiated in November of 2021 and are anticipated to be complete by late Spring 2023. This 
Project Brief describes the work to be undertaken as part of Phase I I: Western  Armourstone  Headland . Future 
repairs as part of Phase II I will be circulated at a later date. The phases of the project are shown below in Figure

 2 SITE DESCRIPTION  
 Location  
The site is located in Humber Bay Park East , along the north shore of  Lake Ontario in  Etobicoke, City of Toronto . 
The closest major  intersection is  Lake Shore Boulevard West and Park Lawn Road  with t he park accessible via 
Marine Pa rade  Drive . The general location of the site and erosion control structures are shown below in Figure 
2.  
 
Figure 2. Map of H umber Bay Park East showing the project location . Source: TRCA, 2019.  
 Land Usage  
Humber Bay Park East  is a public p ark owned by TRCA. The park has formal and informal trails  that run parallel 
to the shoreline  and throughout the park, a publicly accessible beach , and the Air India  182 Memorial . The park 

Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase II – Western Headland  
Project Brief  
 
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    4 
 features a system of streams and ponds, which are currently being revitalized through a separate project led by 
the City of Toronto. The pond  system outlets  at the eastern limit of the western headland (WF11.10).  
 Structure  Description  
2.3.1 Eastern Armourstone Headland (WF11.12)  
The eastern armourstone headland (WF11.12) , seen in Figure 3 in its pre -construction state , is currently under 
repair  as part of Phase I  and the work is anticipated to be completed by spring of  2023. The work involves 
repositioning existing stone and installing new armourstone on top to create a double -layer random placement 
revetment ( Figure 4). The western limit of the structure near the transition to the beach , known as the E astern 
Headland Transition,  is part of the Phase II repair  scope,  and the designs are included in this brief . The transition 
has experienced complete material displacement and significant scouring resulting in damage to a heavily 
utilized informal park trail and presenting a risk to park users ( Figure 5 and Figure 6).

2.3.2 Western Armour stone Headland (WF11.10)  
The western armourstone headland (WF11.10)  seen in  Figure 7 has a str uctural stability rating of “failing ” and 
an overall condition rat ing of “fair” since 2016 as per the ERMP inspection rankings.  Deficiencies of the structure 
are shown in the ERMP sketch below (Figure 8). The headland has an irregular profile due to material 
displacement across the structure ( Figure 9). Large voids  that threaten the overall structure stability have 
developed between stones due to material settling after displacement. Significant scouring due to wave 
overtopping is present along the backshore of the structure placing several park paths and benches at risk , as 
well as presenting a public safety hazard. The eastern limit of the structure  near the transition to the beach , 
known as the Pond O utlet , has experienced major displacement of stone an d is completely outflanked  (Figure 
10 and Figure 11). Rehabilitation of th e Western Armourstone Headland and the  Pond Outlet  will occur during 
Phase II of the Project .

 ANSI/ESA Areas  
There are no Areas of Natural or Scientific Importance (ANSI s) or Environmentally Sensitive Areas  (ESAs)  located

 Typical Slope Dimensions  
The topography of the western headland  is shown in Figure 16. At the western headland, the crest sits around 
the 76 -77 metres above -sea-level (m.a.s.l.) and the parkland behind the structure  sits between 77 and 78 
m.a.s.l. The Pond Outlet and Eastern Head land Transition area s have elevation s between 75 and 76 m.a.s.l.  The 
typical near shore depth is approximately 72 -73 m.a.s.l.

 3 BASELINE INVENTORY  
 Archaeological Assessment  
In 2017 -2018, TRCA Archaeology Staff conducted a Stage 1 Archaeological Assessment in support of the Western 
Waterfront Major Maintenance Strategy. The scope of this assessment encompassed the Humber Bay Park East 
Shoreline  Maintenance Project Area. The  Stage 1  assessment determined that Humber Bay Park East has no 
archaeological potential since it  is a lakefill park that was constructed in the 1970s and 1980s.   
 Aquatic Inventory and Habitat  
TRCA ’s Aquatic Monitoring and  Management ( AMM) group regularly collects fish population  data using 
electrofishing . Four monitoring transects are located near Humber Bay Park East: Southern Beach, East 
Headland, Mimico Creek Mouth and Humber Bay Park West Major Maintenance . The data from these 
monitor ing stations  from 2012 to 2022 is summarized in Table 1. The most common species recorded were 
Alewife , Brown Bullhead , Emerald Shiner,  and White Sucker . Note that these numbers differ from Phase I as  
different monitoring stations have been included . The  East Open Coast st ation included in the Phase I brief is not 
relevant to Phase II and those number s have  been omitted.

identified for removal , and injury and re storation specifications. The Arborist Report is included as a supporting 
document  to this brief.

 Species At Risk  
A Species at Risk (SAR) screening was conducted by TRCA staff. The following SAR have been recorded within the 
project vicinity : 
• American eel ( Anguilla rostrata ) 
• Barn swallow ( Hirundo rustica ) 
• Black snakeroot ( Actaea racemosa ) 
• Eastern wood pewee ( Contopus virens ) 
• Northern map turtle ( Graptemys geographica ) 
• Old field toad flax ( Nuttallanthus canadensis ) 
 
TRCA Fauna Biologist , Paul Prior , was asked to comment on the fauna species identified during the screening. He 
indicated that the habitat requirem ent for the barn swallow and eastern wood pewee are not present at the 
project site. Barn swallow s nest in man -made structures  such as under bridges  and the park’s habitat is 
Humber Bay Park East Shoreline Maintenance Project: Phase II – Western Headland  
Project Brief  
 
Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    17 
 unsuitable for the eastern wood pewee . Despite this it was advised that construct ion begin outside of the 
breeding window  (early April  to late August ) to ensure that there is no disruption to nesting activities. The 
northern map turtle is unlikely to nest in a park with high levels of human disturbance as there are more choice 
habit ats within the Humber River.  
TRCA Flora Biologist , Gavin Miller , was asked to comment about the two observed plant species : old field toad 
flax and black snakeroot. These species are no longer present in the park as of  the latest inventory conducted in 
2014 .  
TRCA Fisheries Biologist , Brian Graham , indicated that there have been no historical American eels caught during 
fisheries monitoring along the open shore side of Humber Bay Park. American eels have only been disco vered in 
the sheltered bays of the area. American eels are also transient in nature  therefore shoreline works are 
expected to have no impact on them. Eels travel along the shoreline to find suitable substrate and would simply 
move on if encountering a work  area. As  predatory fish , they will follow their prey . Once the project has been 
completed, the substrate that will have been placed  for shoreline erosion (including rocks with interstitial 
spaces ) will provide  favo urable  conditions  for eel s. 
Because of th e timing of the project , as well as the nature of the species in the project area, there are no SAR 
concerns for undertaking shoreline works.  
 Soil Characterization  
For the Humber Bay Park East Revitalization Project  undertaken by TRCA’s Restoration and Resource 
Management Group  (RRM ), WSP Canada  (WSP)  was retained to conduct a risk assessment  of the soil 
contamination within Humber Bay Park East. WSP generated three reports in 2020  based on  the findings: a Soil 
Manag ement Plan, a Health and Safety Plan, and a Due Diligence Risk Assessment  Report.  It was discovered 
that the  soils within the Revitalization  Project Area contained many contaminants  that are potentially Health 
and Safety risks for staff  working on -site. A s this risk assessment was undertaken outside of the Shoreline 
Project area, the ERM team undert ook laboratory testing of surface soil  along the shoreline of the Phase I 
project area . The testing and analysis were completed in  Summer 2021 . Ten hand -dug sam ples were taken 0.0 
to 0.4 m below the ground surface and tested for the presence of contaminants. The samples exceeded 
regulation for several metals (including lead, arsenic,  and cadmium) as well as various polycyclic aromatic 
hydrocarbons. However, the contamination levels were not elevated enough to consider the soils to be 
hazardous and they have been classified as non -hazardous under O.Reg 347/90. The results of this analy sis will 
be considered as Health and Safety Plans are developed .

final Phase III works will be submitted once detailed designs are finalized.

Toronto and Region Conservation Authority    |    18
City of Toronto – Urban Forestry Permit  
A Permit to Injure or Destroy Trees will be obtained from the City of Toronto prior to the commencement of 
works  in the Phase II area . Note that the permit will only cover  the Phase II work area and site  access /staging 
locations; separate permitting will be undertaken for any final removals as part of Phase III .  Until this permit is 
received, no trees will be removed from the Phase II work area .  
Additional information on tree removals and injuries  can be found in the Arborist Report . 
Transport Canada – Navigation Protection Program  
Approval is required from Transpo rt Canada for any in-water construction t hat may result in changes or 
introduce hazards to navig ation in navigable water bod ies. Lake Ontario is class ified as navigable waters and the 
Humber Bay Park East area is used by small watercraft. A s this project includes so me underwater features that 
extend into the lake more than 5 metres from the existing shore, an application for approval was submitted to 
Transport Canada under the Navigation Projection Program. These include a submerged stone feature at the 
Pond Outlet and an underwater shoal at the Eastern Headland Transition. Work on these structures will not 
proceed until approved under this Program.
